=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This might be Wallace's best game- it combines the usual tough economics, route-planning, a dry yet solid theme, and multiple ways to score.

The game is divided into two halves: 1)the canal era, and 2)the rail era.  Each turn, you use cards to select two (usually) actions.  You can: take loans (necessary at times unfortunately), place canals/rails (which are required for some shipping ala Age of Steam, and also for scoring purposes), place buildings (can be used for scoring, and needed to do pretty much anything useful), develop/drill down through your building stacks, or ship cotton (if you're set up to do it).  Although the actions are card-driven, because the cards are multi-purpose, it's relatively easy to work around a "bad draw".  

In the first half of the game, you're trying to get some sort of economic engine going, while still trying to scrape some points together.  You need to "flip" your buildings in order to score points for them, and in an interesting co-operative/competitive mechanic, your opponents will often flip them for you.  After the canal era ends, any level 1 buildings, and any canals are removed from the board (after they score points of course).  The rail era is very similar except that rails are more expensive to build, and some buildings may already be on the board.  Another interesting aspect is that start player shifts depending on who spent the least amount of funds each turn.  Scoring occurs at the end of both eras.  

The game only plays 3-4 (I wish it played 2-player, my one complaint of Wallace games), and it is deep and may be perceived as dry.  I personally like the theme for some reason, but it certaintly won't be a draw for many people.  The rules are actually quite clear for once (actually Automobile's were too, so it looks like Wallace is improving in many aspects as a full-time designer).  It's one of those games where it's a grind, but it's an enjoyable grind- a game that needs to get more plays somehow.

RATING: 10
Amazing game.  The rules are positively Byzantine but getting over that, it really is a fantastic game.  Truly well-balanced in that multiple strategies will lead to victory, and the optimal strategy depends not only on your card draw, but also your opponent's actions.  Somebody said in a review that Brass is a game where you develop a number of parasitic relationships with your opponents.  I think that's right on the money and what I like so much about the game.

RATING: 8
1 play / 4 players (3h = 4p)

LIKE: I like the blend of game elements: positioning with turn order, race for map positioning, and race to build specific types of industries. Unique mechanisms and interaction that is not seen in many other games. I really enjoy the subtleties of the foreign and demand markets. I enjoy how there are interesting combos when building and flipping. I like the two eras, it really makes the game unique.

DISLIKE: Incredibly fiddly rules which seem obtuse and not intuitive. The rules between coal and iron are very different and causes a lot of confusion. It may take a couple plays before it smooths out. Sometimes the cards feel random and force a player's strategy.

OVERALL: Great game, very tense. I enjoy the app version as well. The base play is simple, but all the rules overhead makes the game complex and confusing.

After 1st play:
Absolutely fantastic. I kept smiling through the whole game and afterwards everyone was speculating about the outcome. This truly is a sign of a great game. However, being conservative I give Brass a rating of 9. We'll see if it deserves a full ten.


2013/01
Well, Brass didn't earn that 10 but it's still going strong after several plays. I really have to complain about the rulebook but that's the Wallace standard and cannot be helped. Furthermore, the rules are not very intuitive but eventually you'll get a hang of it.

2015-11-04
To my big surprise I sold this in an auction. Might be that I'll regret it later, but at this point of my life I just felt like getting rid of games that haven't seen any or very little play in the past 3-4 years. It seems that nowadays the games hitting the table are action packed sub 90 minute games instead of longer ones like Brass. Moreover, the complexity of the rules in Brass forces an occasional player to reread the rules every single time you want to bring the game into the table. This reduces the chances of Brass getting played. If I only had more space to store my games...

RATING: 8
I've seen reviews saying that Brass is complicated and complex and that one gets overwhelmed by the endless variety of options to win. Oh shut up. While it is obviously no trivial game you would play on Christmas eve together with Granny who just had her traditional glass of punch, it is not as complicated as certain other train-related games. Agreeably, it might take several glasses of punch to understand why coal has to be transported on tracks and why iron is able to "fly" through half the country without ever hitting the occasional airplane (or cotton mill). And agreeably, it actually makes little sense to me that the game is called "Brass". However, as soon as someone else was willing to explain the rules to you, it is easy to grasp (the rule book is supposedly terrible, but not as terrible as the rule books of certain other train-related games). And it is fun to play - don't over-complicate it, just see how far you can go in taking that sweet, sweet money from your opponent who was gullible enough to think that investing in coal was actually a good idea! So really, why don't you sit down, grab some punch, and try another round?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.4
In Brass, the game is divided into 2 phases, the canal phase and the railway phase.  In each of the phase, players can build coal mines, iron mines, shipyards, ports and cotton mills.  In the canal phase, players are able to build canals routes while during the railway phase, players are able to build railway routes.  Besides players can develop their their tiles so that they can accelerate their construction.

When players build cotton mills and ports, they can try to sell cotton making use of each other.  When players build coal or iron mines, players can try to sell them to the market or to use materials to for other purposes.  After ports are used and cotton mills are sold, players earn money and victory points.  Similarly when coal / iron mines are dried out, money and victory points are earned too.  Shipyards offer money and victory points after they are built.  Players can also take loans from the bank.

Good points: It is a great game.  Players can employ different strategies to win the game.  Players can build many canal / railway routes to gain victory points, or build cottons mills and ports to sell cotton to gain money and victory points.  Players can save up enough money to build shipyards which players would be able to have a huge jump in voctory points.

Bad points: Experienced players have an upper hand in the game.  They will be able to know which industry they should build first to get an upper hand.  The game also depends on the cards players drew to guarantee their win.

RATING: 10
This is one of the "funnest" game I have ever played. Imagining myself playing this with 3 other dedicated people makes me have goose-pimples, as it were.
It's the system... I just don't notice time when I play this game; it's the system... it has limitless possibilities for future games.
The cards provide the replayability (helped along by the Distant Market tiles). They give a surprising amount of flexibility without determining your play (opposite in Race for the Galaxy).
The demand and space of the board changes with each game according to what, in what proportion, and when industry tiles are being played; every game has a different narrative.
And the "coopitition" is amazing!
Brass provides all of this and more with so few components. [b]Brilliant game!!![/b]

RATING: 8
It took a fair amount of time for me to wrap my head around what the possibilities really are in the game, but once I did the player interaction dynamics became really fascinating. It's very unique and different than other Euro/Economic hybrid games. 

The only other game like Brass, of course, is Age of Industry (essentially the same game system implemented in a different way). Some of the unique Brass quirks are the more restrictive city cards, the canal/rail phases, and the income bands. All of these along with the very good base map of Brass make it a strong contender for being the best entry in the Brass/AoI system--especially the canal/rail breakup.

However, I like the variability offered by AoI's multiple alternative maps as well as its usage of ships, making coal/iron the same, and opening up cards into regional cards. AoI clicks with me a lot more and just makes me want to play it quite a bit more than I would like to play Brass.

One of my few Always Yes games. Provided it's 4-player. I won't play it at 3 (haven't tried the 2-player variant).

RATING: 9
2-4(3,4)

Still 2p variant only. I think I could play this over and over. It's a bit fiddly (delivery / connection rules, keeping track of spending, etc.), but you get the hang of it. The cleverness of all the available options and how they fit together (there are no "fallback" options ... even taking loans could be a strong play). There are certain guidelines (it's really nice to have coal out, and to have developed through your chaff by the end of canals) but otherwise you have to be very reactive and clever to respond to the ebbs and flows of demand. Especially in things like ensuring that the coal or iron you put out there won't help your opponent!

I don't enjoy lots of multiplayer games with two because the negotiation elements become zero-sum and flattens the strategy (Small World is an example here), but with Brass it doesn't, somehow, feel that way. It feels like Wallace has turned the very clever trick of making these sorts of decisions mutually beneficial and it's up to each player to individually consider who benefits best. Great economic game.

RATING: 6
There are several similarities between Brass and Age of Steam - the building of networks, the shipping of goods, loans and a frequently changing turn order.  However, the two games are actually very different.  Brass has a more forgiving economy and balances network building against industrial development. The cards work nicely to bring a bit of focus to players' turns and to reduce their options to manageable proportions.  

Brass seems highly thematic. However, there are a few oddities.  Like iron not needing to be shipped by rail, railways needing no iron to be built, and there being no money to be earned in the transport business.  Several rules feel like patches to fix things that didn't quite work in game terms.

The rules might be the worst set that Warfrog has produced, with the main body lacking both clarity and detail, and then a reference section adding more rules and the missing details.  Combine this with lots of niggly exceptions and some novel systems, and this game has a pronounced learning curve.  I haven't managed to get this game to table with any regularity, so that curve has been slogged up each time.  What has become apparent over a few plays is that the kinder economy of Brass (compared to Age of Steam) has been achieved at some cost to variability.

RATING: 8

The game is not luck-free but it IS less random than Railways of the World for example. All in all a very competitive game with a nice economy system.

RATING: 7
Update: Dropped to 7 again. Doesn't get played very much anymore.
Update: This is one of those games where I think about all sorts of different strategies for days after a game, but then really struggle to come up with a coherent plan during play. Having resolved a couple of rules issues (fingers crossed. But all the answers are there in the rulebook folks) I have raised my rating from a 7 to a 9. It really is a challenge to keep on top of the possibilities, while watching what your opponents are up to and avoiding wasting a single action. My initial issue with the rather arbitrary nature of the cards and distant market track has disappeared. They ensure flexibility and variety in play.

Difficult to rate at this stage as it is testing my tolerance threshold for the amount of "stuff going on" I can face at any one time. At this point I cannot tell whether all the options and limitations feel valid, or some are just a superficial distraction. Plus there still seems to be the odd minor rule misunderstanding we need to iron out. Anyway I will say it is definitely a good game with lots of planning and strategic positioning, combined with tactical choices and interaction. It could well be a great game.

RATING: 9
April 2012:  I have played a number of "gamers' games," which are so complex in rules and/or gameplay and/or theme that they clearly have very limited appeal to the general public and/or the casual, occasional gamer.

As I see it, Brass plays as a HARDCORE gamers' game, essentially at the top of the gamers' game perch, up there with Goa.

With Brass, I find it slightly amusing to consider just how awful Brass would play if an innocent, unsuspecting person bought it at the local game store, brought it home, tried to make heads or tails of the convoluted (though logical) rules, then played a first game with ALL first time Brass players. What a bleeping disaster!   And so, my first game of Brass would have been a discouraging experience had I not first watched the BGG video on it, AND played with 3 experienced Brass gamers. 

So then...why is Brass...well...good? As a euro gamer I am actually VERY impressed with Brass.

Brass is good because Wallace has managed to design a complex game, presenting a complex gaming challenge, with multiple layers of competition and cooperation, on a spatially very interesting board, that is very deeply imbued with his historical theme.

And if you suspend your desire for "mechanics fireworks"...there are, in fact, some very interesting mechanics used in Brass. The turn order being determined by the amount of money spent is quite interesting to think about. The income track is non-linear, which I found to be mysteriously interesting...clearly the product of a lot of play testing. The tile flipping mechanism is interesting enough...something to consider constantly in the game...though incredibly fiddly for the first time gamer.

But...it was the card play mechanism that I found most interesting in Brass...you play two...then draw 2...then have a wonderful "endgame feeling" of eventually dropping your hand down from 8 cards to zero, two cards at a time. That "endgame mechanism" really worked for me, particularly with the announced warning of..."no loans in the final four rounds"... creating very interesting Power Grid type money challenges.

In fact, the whole card aspect of Brass really worked for me last night...though I wonder if I was lucky and had some good card draws, and in future plays, I might curse the card mechanism. I wonder.

Lastly, the closeness of the scores in my game, with each one of us pursuing different approaches and strategies, clearly shows an incredibly well tested game. Amazing how close those scores were. Happy to take the win on my first play!  

Giving this an 8 of 10.

November 2012:  After several more plays, somewhat grudgingly, I must concede that Brass deserves a 9...it is that well designed, and interesting to play, for gamers like me.

Raising it to a 9 of 10.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
What an original game !

The relationship between the different "resources" of the game (cotton, coal, steel, money, victory points) give the player a lot of interesting choices to make.

Furthermore, another level of inter relations appears between the players because the industries they build may be used by everyone (coal, steel, canals or rails, ports).

Even the cities you choose to build in will affect the value in points of the canals/rails links that connect to it.

Another interesting aspect is the change between the 2 eras when canals disappear and railways start to be built. The industries of greater technology stay on the board and the lower tech buildings disappear.  The result is that you have to plan to establish level 2 tech buildings before the rail era as they will serve as a base for your development for the second half of the game.

A great game and it has a superb 2 player variant here on BGG.

RATING: 8
Oct 2012 update: Playing this further, I'm liking the game more.  The excellent two-player variant helps, but so also does some further understanding of the thematic reasons behind some of the rules.  Points are prestige, which is some sort of representation of long-term income potential and/or influence in society (i.e. you're trying to be knighted).  Iron is not iron ore but a one-time infusion of machinery, engines, locomotives, etc., which is why it doesn't need to be transported like coal.  The game is still a bit too abstract, and a bit overcomplicated, but underneath that is a good game.

2009 comment: Every time I play this game I am less impressed.  It's an abstract victory point collection game with a fairly thin economic theme.  This is not fatal, but with the numerous exceptions to the rules, it's a game that's both overlong and not as much fun as many other economic games.

RATING: 8.5
This Wallace title is a return to the Age of Steam style economic engine game.  The play consists of building up cotton mills, coal mines, harbors and ship yards to earn points at 2 scoring points.  Game play is quite straight forward, but it will take a round or two to get your head around it.  Once it clicks though, the game play is actually quite fast.  The mechanics work well together.  Player interaction is limited to interaction on the board (vs directly between players) similar in feel to Age of Steam.  Unlike age of Steam though - this game is not about connections - its about building your industry in the best places.  The connections are secondary (but still important).  The only draw backs I see is the somewhat ackward clearing of the boardduring the transition from canal to rail (1st period to 2nd period)  and the odd 'teleporting' steel vs 'railcar' coal.  Game length (shorter than Age of Steam) is just about perfect for the game's depth.  Easy recommendation.

RATING: 7
Kb. fél évet vártam rá, hogy kipróbáljam ezt a játékot. Többen is mondták, hogy a szabályt elolvasva inkább félretették, emiatt kicsit féltem tőle. De annyira nem ijesztő a rendszere, inkább az a baj, hogy nagyon rosszul van megírva. Ami sajnálatos egy ilyen jó játék esetén.
A játék tele van kivételekkel, mintha folyamatosan lett volna javítgatva az alapmechanizmus. Vagy talán a valóságot akarta a szerző minél élethűbben modellezni. Tetszik a 3 féle termék teljesen eltérő mechanizmusa. A kikötőben vásárolt szén/acél kicsit a Power Gridre emlékeztet. Amire nagyon érdemes odafigyelni, az a sajátos hitelfelvételi mód, ami szerintem elég nagy taktikázási lehetőségeket rejt magában.
Mindjárt az első játékunkban előfordult az az állítólag ritka eset, hogy teljesen elfogyott a szén, így muszáj volt felülépíteni egy épületet.
A szerencsefaktor a kártyák húzásánál végig jelen van a játékban (a 2 kártyás szuperakció enyhít talán a szerencse szerepén) és a pamuteladásnál pedig még hangsúlyosabban jelenik meg. De ennyi belefér szerintem.
Összességében jó játék, de több játék kell, hogy jól megismerje az ember.
Egyébként tökéletesen nyelvfüggetlen.

M.Wallace jatekainak egyik ekkove. 
A jatek megkoveteli az elore tervezest, es annak tudatos vegrehajtasat. Ad hoc otlettol vezerelt lepesek nem igazan vezetnek eredmenyre. Sok mindenre figyelni kell.
A jatek szabalya hosszu, osszetett, es itt tenyleg minden reszlet nagyon fontos. A jatekido egy kicsit hosszu (annak ellenere, hogy teljesen lekoti a jatekosokat es igy hamar elrepul a kozel 3 ora). RATING: 9
Jul-27-2008
First time playing the game.  I really like it.  The game is very unique to me because I think this is the only game that I actually don't feel bad when my opponents take my resources or when I flip their industries that give them points and income.  It has a very unique cooperative feel.  It is very evocative of its theme and I really feel like I was building up Lancashire with my friends, yet competing against them.  It is fun analyzing the board to see which market (links, coal, iron, cotton, ports) is there for me to take advantage on.  It is cool to provide that service to my opponents and be rewarded for them.  I like the 2 eras which give very distinct feels.  I think the rules certainly could have been more streamlined like Age of Steam but I think those extra exceptions are worth it.  The rules are actually intuitive, the teacher just has to make sure to give the thematic reason for the rules.  It helps a lot on remembering the rules.  Obviously, having printed player aids for everyone helps a lot.  Thanks to BGG I was able to have a very successful first game playing Brass.   I really like this game so far and really want to play it again a lot.
Tentative Rating: 9

RATING: 8.5
What a beast of a game! After one play I was astonished by the elegance of the design, and how every rule fits and everything flows together (I don't mind the few exceptions: they're annoying to explain to first timers, but everything clicks quite naturally).
It is a brainburning and immersive experience, driven by cards that dictate play without being so dominant: with a hand of 8 cards you can "see" 4 turns in the future, and with only one of the actions that stick to the cards, you have plentiful of options for playing or dumping cards.
What amazes me the most, is the elegance of the demand and offer built in the system, together with the set of incentives to build canals/rails (knowing that other can benefit before you...). In addition, I love the two eras, with the first being just a preparation step for the second...
Can't wait to play it again!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
The Kickstarter gave me incentive to get a few plays of this in. Brass takes a few games to get a hold of the rules, and then a few more games to get a hold of some of the beginning strategies. Deep and replayable. The supply and demand mechanism is quite good, and flipping tiles is satisfying. There's also a good amount of positive interaction between the players--just try not to leave building/shipping opportunities open to your opponents. 

This game, however, will not be for every play group or gathering, and will be better for a group that can give it consistent plays. For that, I've set my rating at an 8.

Edit, Aug 2017: After playing a few more times it is clear that Brass is simply a great strategy game. The lack of the current version having a good 2 player variant keeps this game from moving to a 9+ game. The new Roxley version could fix that.

RATING: 10
I find the cards a little annoying.  Even though I understand that the cards are multi-purpose, such that you can discard non-useful ones for loans and for building Canals/Rails, I disliked drawing certain cards in the Canal stage and not drawing certain cards during the Rail stage.  For some reason, my mind is always fuzzy when I play Brass.  I keep forgetting what causes iron to be added to the board, taken away, etc.

Update: After a few sessions of Brass, the small number of quirky rules have begun to stick to my brain better.  The cards still dictate your options (unless you don't mind spending 2 cards for 1 action), but that can be said of most games that feature cards. 

2nd Update:  Brass has become one of my favorite games, which is surprising since I wasn't that impressed the first few times that I played.     

2-player, 6 plays!
3-player, 27 plays!
4-player, 16 plays!    


[b] Catalonia Map[/b]
During the canal aka "Road" era, play is heavily focused on Barcelona.  I'm not sure it's viable to start elsewhere.  Unfortunately most of the coal mines are located far to the west, which makes coal very expensive during the Rail era.  I do like that there are 3 shipyard locations during the "Road" era.  Interested in more plays.

Update:  I have grown very fond of this map.    

3-player, 29 plays!
4-player, 14 plays!

RATING: 8
First play: Unfortunately played by the wrong rules and so had a strange game but enjoyed it none the less.

Subsequent game: Rules are now under control but the rule book is a lesson in disjointed thought and would benifit from a redraft. Once you get a handle on the game structure, the flow is quite logical, however players must be very disciplined in the way they conduct their moves. Its very easy to get something wrong, miss some available coal, pay too much money, need to retrace your steps, forget to take a card, leave iron on the market, return money to the bank rather than the allocated squares etc etc. Someone who understands the game backwards needs to Police each player vigilantly or too late 2 people have taken their turn and you remember that the income phase was missed.

However hard the struggle the destination is worth the sweat. An excellent game.

I think my rating will rise with more plays.

RATING: 8.2
Deep economic game as only Wallace can make them. Very hard too learn from the rules and a very steep learning curve. But once the game clicked I had a great time playing it. I can even imagine it becoming a quick game if played with experienced players. The cards tend to limit your options but I think that's a good thing. Without the limits the game would be too overwhelming. Different roads to victory seem to be well balanced. Themewise it workes allthough there are some rough edges in there. Esspecially the loans seem very strange. They generate tons of cash and don't really have any downside to them. If you're into deep euros you should definitely give this one a try!

RATING: 9.9
My favorite Industry / Manufacturing game.  I won the first game I played during a gaming convention.  After the canal phase i was in last place, 11 VPs behind the leader because I didn't realize that the canals scored VPs based on the number indusrtries they connect.  The key to winning is using technology to circulate through the low level industries.  Its an excellent game that I believe is forgiving.

2 July 2010:  Played a three player game against Bruce and Bob and won with 204 points.  This was an unusual game because I won with only one rail played in the rail era.  I played over 3 of my counters one coal in the canal era and two ports in the rail era.  I made 36 points on my next to last turn and another 21 points on my last turn.  One of the most satisfying gaming wins I have ever had given that my competition have played the game 50 times to my 10 times and the strategy I choose was unique.

RATING: 7

RATING: 8.2
A game about the mills, canals, rails and other industrial goings on that took place in the north of England. I've played this a few times now, which has allowed me to learn some of the intricacies of the game. There is a nice change of tack between canals and rails, which gives two game phases with scoring after each. I like the "flip the chit" scoring of buildings giving you income and VP's on the back, with build costs and resources required or provided situated on the front. Brass is a different take on the "connect places up and ship things about" mechanism that games which let you lay track on a board usually come up with.

RATING: 7
아직까지 큰 재미요소를 찾지 못했다....
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This economic themed game is one of Martin Wallace's most interesting additions to the Warfrog line. It recreates the industrialization of Lancashire as players build industries (Cotton Mills, Coal Mines, Iron Foundries, Ports and Shipyards) around which canals then rail lines are networked. Cotton goods are sold and resources (coal, machinery) consumed in the building process which provides both income and victory points. In the end, the most victory points (Prestige) amassed determines the winner with left over money providing a minor bonus.

The card deck portrays either the various industries that can be built or locations in Lancashire which can house them. The play of a card must accompany each action the player wishes to perform: build, develop technology, sell cotton goods, take out loans or construct canal or rail links. The deck is tailored to provide enough cards for eight rounds of play for four players. Each round consists of the play of two cards permitting two actions (except for the first round of the game which is restricted to one card play). Once the deck is exhausted and all players have used all their cards, the initial "Canal Period" ends and victory points are scored and recorded. The deck is reshuffled and a further eight rounds constitutes the "Rail Period" after which a further set of Victory points are determined and added to each player's previous total.

Thus actions are limited (typical of Martin's designs) and deciding how to derive the maximum benefit from those provided each player is the heart of the game. There are a number of different way to accumulate VPs and it will require several playings for players to see the linkages and dynamics that this game provides. It appears that this will be a game best played by players of equivalent talent and experience as it will be very unforgiving toward novices.

The game is well produced, colourful and quite thematic and is one of my favourite Martin Wallace designs. I think it will prove to have considerable staying power and will serve to increase Martin's already strong reputation for thoughtful, thematic and challenging games of strategy.

Edit Note: This game has proven to be one of the strongest multi-player strategy game ever designed. Online play is vigorous and there is also an iPad app available. Of all the games I own I keep coming back to this one.

RATING: 6
Feel I should like this more than I do. It's an economic game, and I like economic games, it's got multiple routes to victory, development - what's not to like?

RATING: 9.4
Oh crap!  I have four coppers and 5 of my 8 cards are ports.  WTH!

I love this game.  This game is played to earn Vps which are figured 2 times.  Both times at the ends of "periods".

The periods are firstly the "canal" period and secondly the "rail" period.  During each of these periods of growth you will spend several cycles called player actions doing one of 5 differing things.  

These 5 activities in each player's cycle will keep you thinking, doing math, and hoping for good cards the next time you get to draw.

It is a very tight game and much easier to play than the rules would let on.

There is plenty of math, enough rules to keep things lively, and luck, and medium leveled player interaction to make this a very intricate and nifty game.

So for me, it is definitely not a game for everyday, but one a might play from time to time - in the right mood.

UPDATE: The game really grows on you, if you play it more. 6 -> 7.5 -> 8.5

RATING: 8
Oh, Martin Wallace, why are you so mean to your fans? Yet another brilliant design, perfectly calibrated, brilliantly executed, and maddeningly frustrating to play. If there is a game that could ever provoke a couple to open divorce proceedings, chances are it was designed by Martin Wallace.

As in all of his games, the number of choices you have in the first turn are daunting, and one bad move early on can ruin you completely. On the other hand, as you play your first game (badly), you begin to realize what you [i]should[/i] have done last turn, and despite your misery you begin to really appreciate the design of the game.

The best part about Brass is that you not only have to start planning from turn one, but you also have to be adaptable. If someone starts to capitalize on the distant markets, build some ports. If someone begins to heavily develop their shipyards, forget about your own shipyards and build an iron foundry to make a quick profit. It really is a great game. You're just miserable for every minute of it.

RATING: 8
A real gamers game this one, as we've come to expect from the Warfrog Wallaces, and not for the faint hearted but as fresh and innovative as any of his best - and, while it will take more plays to see if it is amongst them, I am already hopeful.  The now familiar 'build up income/then score victory points' structure is in here, though well disguised, indeed hidden, by the rules.  What marks it as a Wallace and lifts it beyond the norm is the intimate connection with the physical geography - which adds an extra layer (or two) to the game.  I like it and look forward to playing again. Long on first play but we completed the second half of the game in an hour which should mean that the two hours timing on the box isn't far wrong.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.99
This is a fabulous game ... once you get past the rule book. Why wouldn't a game about Cotton Mills, Coal Mines and Ports in 19th Century Lancashire not be an instant hit! Seriously the industrial revolution theme of this game is superb, you really feel like one of those pioneering entrepreneurs rushing to get the best business and do the best deals before one of the competition does.

The mechanic for trading is a little odd at first, but once you get the hang of it the challenge begins. You have to plan ahead, but be flexible enough to react what's going on around you. This might require you to respond to certain cards or lack thereof, but more likely it's about a competitor grabbing the spot you wanted, or you may see that they have a perfect opportunity that you might need to block, or the coal and/or iron market might have suddenly shot up or down.The mechanic for chaging the turn order is excellent and not to be overlooked. 

This game absolutely rewards the time to learn it! Would be a 9.999 if you could play with 5. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
UPDATE: The good news: Brass creaked to 5 plays. The bad news: that looks like the end of the line.

UPDATE: And the rating keeps falling ...

I've kind of enjoyed playing Brass; so the rating is unlikely to fall any further, even though I am unlikely to play again. But the nail in Brass's coffin for me was when I realized just how totally, gratuitously opaque the game is; the same problem of wandering, pointless difficulty that Age of Steam had. The game is won on VPs, but those VPs are needlessly abstract. What do VPs represent in this game of the industrial age? Nothing. Not prestige; not earning power; not net worth; nothing. Just one of the perverse effects this has is that cash in hand is worth VPs, but actual earnings are not; so a player with $60 in hand but who is earning nothing has more actual victory points than a player who earns $60 each turn but currently has nothing on hand. Um... Otherwise, this is just an obscure exercise in figuring out whether canals/rails, textile factories, coal mines, or whatever represents a better way to get VPs, with absolutely no way to get an intuitive handle on it. You just have to get out the spreadsheet and crunch the numbers, which of course incidentally results in endless, painful downtime. It's not a game about shipping textiles, digging up coal in Lancashire during the industrial revolution, or anything like that; it's a game about doing complicated optimization math, with a theme that is as thin as any Michael Schacht game.

Anyway, this is a classic Martin Wallace game: clever underlying ideas, but dysfunctional realization (and atrocious rules, not just badly written but also more exceptions and edge cases than actual rules, I think). Give me Container or El Capitan, two economic games that came out at about the same time, over this underdeveloped mess.
---
UPDATE: Curse you, Martin Wallace. Hire a damn developer already, will you? Yet another bleeping game with good ideas and no polish. I like this better than most of his games, but it's still much to rough. I guess the Martin Wallace groupies will guarantee sales even if he never learns to refine his games. A shame.
---
I enjoyed the first play, but the second was just OK. So the rating slides from 8 to 7. It's fun but a pretty much by-the-numbers Wallace design at this point. The good news is that it seems like a much tighter design than what he usually puts out: the various industries seem ballpark-balanced with respect to each other, and the game plays cleanly, at least by Wallace standards (you still wouldn't mistake this for a professionally designed German offering though).

The downside I think is the length combined with the lack of real game development. Brass is 3 hours and, it seems to me, a somewhat repetitive 3 hours. Also, it's susceptible to being struck down by both analysis paralysis and by mistakes in play by one player giving large windfalls arbitrarily to another. As for development, the game doesn't move along that much, the endgame is pretty similar to the opening game. This combined with the fixed and somewhat restrictive geography and industry mixes may be a problem for replayability. Oh, and despite being a fairly straightforward game, the rules are a mess.

I don't think the downsides are hugely significant, Brass is a pretty clever game system, but I do think the length may ultimately be a problem, and combined with the replayability concerns and high price-point, it's probably a try-before-you-buy for most folks.

RATING: 7
While the game seems kind of clunky and unwieldy at first (there are quite a few odd and unintuitive rules to remember) this one really started to shine once we got the hang of it.  There are several strategies to pursue, with a little tactical maneuvering throughout the game to top it off as you respond to what other players are doing.  

After more plays this one really shines.  Once you get over the hurdle of getting the rules down, it's a really great game.  There are many different ways to go about winning, with plenty of tactical choices to make as you react to the other players' moves.  The mean drawback is that I fear too many people will be put off by the rules before they get to the point of loving the game, meaning I'm not going to get to play this one as much as I'd like!

The only reason Brass isn't higher for me is that Age of Industry has now come along, and will see the majority of play over this.  Still a great game, but Age of Industry is just more streamlined and open-ended for expansion maps to keep things interesting.

RATING: 9
Updated 5/14/10.

It took a while, but I think we've finally managed to wrap our heads around this game.  What we've found has been truly awesome.  

Let me get the bad out of the way first: (1) It took five or six games before we played one with all the right rules.  For some reason, I found the rule book impenetrable, and it is certainly not structured in a way that makes it handy as a reference guide. (2) The fiddly and asymmetric rules also feel a bit gamey, and the game is very hard to introduce to a new player (the length doesn't help).  (3) Even if you can get a new player to the table, it's a game that is so open-ended that it's hard for a beginner to know what to do.  When do I build what?  Where do I build?  Does it matter what I build?  Why? 

Now for the good: It's awesome.  The components are great.  The artwork sets the mood appropriately and gameplay is strongly thematic:  I feel like an opportunistic captain of industry.  Although I've been mostly playing with the well-respected two-player variant, I had another taste of the three-player game recently, and the difference is astonishing.  This is not a game meant to be played with less than three (2 is still fun, but it's just not the same).

After so many games, I think there is still the potential for more: I've not yet learned the value of building in certain areas, or when it makes sense to build over my own industries, etc.  But it is already a really incredible, intense game of head-to-head-to-head competition that is right up my alley.

RATING: 5
[7/11/2007] After merely one game, I can't say I'm truly getting the various strategies involved in the game.Yet I'm pretty sure Brass won't ever appeal to me as well as Age of Steam, Princes of the Renaissance or Struggle of Empires. Gameplay just doesn't seem edgy and interactive enough. Pretty dry.

As a result, not that much fun, really. I miss the spark of inspiration I immediately saw in the before-mentioned games.That said, I do want to play it again in order to get a better grip of the strategies involved. But I'm confident it won't ever earn high marks with me.

I personally find Age of Steam's economic model far more interesting as you initially run a deficit and strive to break even. Also, money seems less tight in Brass as the game progresses. 

Also, positional play on the board seems more interesting in AoS than in Brass. More of an exciting race to build the most routes. Also, in AoS you often still can wheedle yourself into a city - albeit at a higher cost - whereas in Brass you might find yourself entirely blocked. Of course, the location cards might help you out here, but that's wholly up to your card draws...

Moreover, the determination of one's turn order seems to me far more interesting in AoS. The auctions really add so much to the game. Brass's expenditure-related turn-order seems far less interesting and I don't really appreciate expenditure manipulation to that end being one of the strategic decisions involved. Just feels fiddly, articificial and lacking true depth to me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Planning, planning, planning. You need to plan out why you are doing something. Because of how elements interrelate, you need to know how this action you are performing is going to turn into end game points, otherwise, why are you performing it? Very little generates points on it's own. Also, a lot of the generation of points occurs due to an interaction with the interests of others, something you need to be keenly aware of while playing. The canal period and how it interacts with the rail period is a piece of brilliance.

However, this game has all of these little finicky rules that are difficult to remember and get right. That is unfortunate.

The theme is also not my favourite. The industrial revolution, particularly in this area, was about the exploitation and abuse of the lower classes by people with money. The industrial revolution created a class of people who lost their jobs because they were replaced by machines, and then "hired" their children, to work in their cotton mills. "Hired" being a loose term when you tell starving children that they can eat if only they thumbprint and mark an X on this piece of paper they can't read which stipulates how they'll be working their next fifteen years without parole and two paydays a year (with pay being about enough to buy a sandwich). They usually started at around five or six and worked sixteen hour shifts. Sixteen hour shifts? How's that possible. I've seen five year olds, they sleep way too much to do that. Yes, that's why they had to hire adults to beat them when they started to get drowsy. The whole theme is horrific, and is often the case with boardgames, we play as the monsters and act as if they were heroes, erasing the victims from the story.

RATING: 9
Awesome stuff going on with quite a bit of hand management and board manuevering.  My favorite aspect of the game is that you want to win, but you also want to try and entice everyone else into helping you flip your buildings.  Such a clever way to give the feeling of working-together.  The Amun-Re style board reset is also very interesting lends a depth to the strategy in an otherwise tactical game.  The rules can be a bit hard to grasp and will definately make you say 'Wait, why can't I do that again?' for the first game, but it quickly falls in place and makes sense.  I think Brass is to Age of Industry much the same way Age of Steam is to Steam --- the 'remake' of both of these games lacks some of the gritty depth that make the games shine brightly.

RATING: 8
(11/2017 update). Played again after many years. It clicked this time and didn't feel clunky. I appreciated the canal era as being an opportunity to establish board position. The rail era had none of the issues I had qualms about. upgraded to an 8

(10/4/10 Update) I'm still not convinced about that game. I feel like the canal period is a bit of a waste of time since a) it is only about a third of your score and most of your infrastructure disappears. And the 'network' building in the rail period is oddly inverted. Because rails don't produce income, no one builds them aggressively until they have decent income generated elseways. This seems counter to the concept of your typical network theory where there a positive feedback loop that creates incentive to improve the network to increase the value of the nodes which makes the network more valuable, etc. Instead, the big rail boom at the end - which can account for more the 50% of your score  in that period - is not really valuable as a network but merely as an efficient transformer of money into points.

(Original)I need to play this again at least once to have an adequate take. In the meantime, my impression was that I like what (I think) Wallace is trying to do - meld a supply/demand economic engine to a network building game; kind of Container meets Early Railways - but it feels too clunky. Besides a very quirky ruleset, the gameplay felt just a little too... awkward.

RATING: 7
[UPDATE 3/16: While this game has a warm place in my heart, I must confess that fifty-odd times in I am starting to feel a bit burned out on it -- not that I have mastered it by ANY stretch of the imagination. Still a great game, but when you find yourself passing on it in favor of stuff you don't rate a 10, or even a 9, maybe it's time for a little re-think. It will probably pass, but this one's just not hot for me right now.]

:tobacco: :( :) :) :D It's tricky to learn but not as fierce as its reputation. Either the rules were updated before I got it or other people have a much lower tolerance for mediocre rulebooks than I do -- regardless, those who power through will find an incredible experience. The ways in which your actions open up new possibilities for other players that you can extract some benefit from is just boggling, and other games with this mechanic like Caylus can't quite compare (though Caylus is very, very good.)

When it comes to the various niggling exceptions and oddities and special rules on the board, it's best to imagine them explained to you not by Martin Wallace but by the late Craig "Ironhead" Heyward, former NFL running back and liquid soap pitchman, e.g.: "But Ironhead, why do I need canal or rail links to transport coal but not iron?" "Shut up, chump. Coal represents bulk quantities of heavy stuff that must be continually provided to factories in order for them to operate efficiently and iron cubes represent one-time capital investment in relatively easy to transport components like machine tools!" "But Ironhead, why do I never have to pay my loans back?" "Shut up, chump. Loans represent things like stock issues that give you a short-term cash injection at the cost of long-term profitability?" "Ironhead, what's with this thingie?" "That's the virtual link between Liverpool and Birkenhead. It prevents one player from locking everyone else out from building a shipyard in Birkenhead just because they built in Ellesmere Port." "But Ironhead..." "Just play the damn game, fool."

Best with 4, only slightly less wonderful with 3. Haven't tried the 2 player because I rarely get a chance to play two player games and would rather pull out one of my two-player only games.

RATING: 9
I've played this game a number of times now and it continues to get better and better.  For an economic game without resources to fight over or auctions to win I find the player interaction very compelling.  The game art is fantastic, invoking the early 1800s.  All components are top notch except the money, but we'd rather use poker chips anyway.  One aspect I enjoy is that no matter whether you play with 3 or 4 players the game is excellent and plays differently.  

I like games with lots of paths to victory and this is certainly a game that fits that bill.  The cards add a dynamic element to the game that will narrow your choices, which are further narrowed down as the board fills up with industries.  I cannot say enough good things about this excellent game.

RATING: 9
I've only played twice so far. I tend to enjoy several of Martin Wallace designs, so I had high hopes for Brass. So far, the jury is still out for me. 

The production values are exactly what I'd expect from a Warfrog game. The card quality is just fine, but not equal to some of the high end Euro cards. 

I like the 2 phases of the game (canal and rail). That creates a neat element. Its a bit of a dilemma to build things that are easier, can score now...vs things that are harder but will stay around for the 2nd half of the game.

Taking loans is a LOT less punishing than I expected it to be. It becomes a no-brainer, since there is really not a good way to generate enough income through normal play to remain competitive. I was disappointed in that.

Most disappointing though, is the card play itself. I felt like my hand restricted what I could do on the map to a large degree. Maybe it was lack of imagination, but I found myself in the 2nd half of the game with no cards that I could use to create synergy with the network I'd already created in the first half. It felt like an artificial restriction, and I had a hard time finding a thematic explanation that took away the frustration. 

One of the things I enjoy about Age of Steam is that you can use creativity to do lots of things, pretty much anything you can afford and can think of. Here, the loans cost actions and the cards restrict your building, both changes that I like less than Age of Steam. I realize Brass is its own game, but I rate is a notch below the best Martin Wallace games I've played.

I need to play Brass more to figure out if there is more that I haven't caught onto yet. It fell pretty flat with the local group (in a game where I wasn't present) so may be hard to get on the table.

RATING: 9
Brass is a hard game to describe. It's one part economic system, one part hand management, one part cooperation and one part screw job. At the most basic level, players are trying to build industries, and then to use the material produced by those industries up so they can flip the tile over and score points. To use materials, ports are often useful, which are also player determined. Finally, there are shipyards, which are basically like the big buildings in Puerto Rico.

The entire game is driven by a card system that only seems to matter for the last couple of rounds in each half of the game, as you normally have 8 cards to choose from, and they only matter when you're doing one of the five possible actions, building a building. The game is divided into halves, and the first half seems to be mainly about building up a basic infrastructure so you can score beaucoup points in the second portion.

Like many of Martin's games, grokking this out of the gates can be difficult, in particular here because the 3 goods types, Cotton, Coal, and Iron each have different rules associated with how they move and how they are sold. The rules for building industries also vary in a major way depending on whether one is using a location card to build them, or an industry card. So the niggly rules are a hurdle here, probably a solid 1 game hurdle at that, as we were just getting used to them by the end of our first trip to Lancashire.

At its heart though, I think this is a very interesting economic game where different strategies seem viable. The first game was extremely overwhelming because of all the niggly rules and extra subsystems. By the second, things were definitely coming together though, and I was able to pursue an actual strategy in the game. Now, several more games in, the game continues to impress me based on the number of viable strategies.

The key to doing well in Brass is related to real business: you want to be in the under-served market, because everything comes down to getting your buildings flipped. Being the only person in something like coal or iron can mean that other players are forced to flip your buildings for you, which means you can score even more points doing other stuff. Taking loans out can be a powerful way to finance the second half, but it comes at the expense of actions, which could be used by a stingier operator to put up inexpensive buildings, and thus to generate a permanent income which will open up more total building (i.e.: VP generating) actions over the course of the game. I have won using a huge variety of strategies: heavy loan, no loan, rail baron, shipyard centred, &c. Each path seems to be potentially viable depending on what the other players are up to, this is the mark of a great game.

The game seems to also scale well, playing equally well with 3 and 4 players. Vintage Wallace in any case, and a welcome change of pace from his continuous combat-driven releases in recent years. Started at an 8, presently at a 9. My only long-term concern is sameyness.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I consider this and Steam some of the best games I've ever played, Martin Wallis has popped up on my radar.  All I now have to do now is schedule a game of Steam or perhaps Ages of Steam.

RATING: 7
My first experiences with Brass were not entirely positive. I recognised that there was a lot of 'game' present, but tough explanations, lots of little rules, and unfamiliarity with the concept of logistics hampered my playing pleasure. In the first (now deleted) comment I also thought I recognised a growth aspect in Brass: while there certainly is an element of industry becoming more expensive and yielding more VPs it is far from the core of the game. The core however [i]is[/i] about finding opportunity in an emerging network. As far as I can make out players do not really [i]need[/i] each other; it's just that matters are financially cheaper if you use what others have on offer. But of course there is a catch in that you help the other gain VPs though this cannot really be avoided because of the proximity rules. What I find more difficult to explain / use is overbuilding, but I'm sure that repeated plays will show why it is useful, or even necessary.

In any case, in the years after I played Brass first my understanding of and appreciation for logistics and the like grew, and when I revisited the game at some point I couldn't for the likes of me think of a reason why I [i]wouldn't[/i] like the game. So I forked out cash for a copy, read the rules (understood them perfectly too), played it, and as per expectation great game. My opponents, who remembered an arduous whole, liked it a lot better too. Perhaps due to improved understanding of games in general, but also perhaps due to a smoother and confident explanation. Given the positive sentiment and the enthusiasm in seeing this game hit the table more often the rate has been upgraded, with pleasure, with clear opportunity to be raised further in case the game patterns turn out to be sufficiently diverse.

I find it interesting to also reflect on my altered attitude towards the cards. Long ago I thought the game very dependent on what you got, but that turns out to be not really the case. Only at the very last rounds of either era do you run into constraints, and this might work out to be too much windfall. But I doubt you'd win or lose the game because of this; if scores are close then it really was too close to call, and if scores were wide apart then you did better (or worse) during the game where you had plenty of opportunity.

That all said, I still find the artwork unbelievably yucky, though. Stark, washed-out, all function and no form, and cramped. Perhaps I ought to invest in flat wooden squares to physically raise industries which haven't yet been flipped off the board.

RATING: 7.6
Typical Martin Wallace, but I couldn't help feeling like it's over-complicated.

In short, players are building industries with limited resources, with a need to have routes built between the cities.  Building up money quickly is essential because it gets harder to earn later in the game.  Maybe you'll want to take a loan for a short-term gain to build the industries you want?

While I appreciate the use of theme and the feeling of tight decisions each turn, this game just feels a bit too fiddly for my tastes (and the rulebook is needlessly confusing).  Had I discovered it years ago I might have felt differently, but now I know there are other Wallace games that do things better.

RATING: 8

Game scales really well with 3 or 4 players. 3 tends to have a smaller transport network, more wide open map and higher scores, while 4 has networks that build quickly but lots of competition for building real estate and lower scores. 4 is more chaotic but both are fun when you adjust your play accordingly.

RATING: 8
A great economic game with the usual idiosyncratic Martin Wallace rules. It can be difficult to pick up at first since Wallace tries hard to model the particular economy in a particular place at a particular time, but once you get it down it simply works. A better game than Age of Industry mainly due to the canal/rail era split.

BTW this is exhibit number 1 showing how Martin creates systems in service of a theme, unlike Stefan Feld--who seems to create mechanisms just to create mechanisms and then somehow find a theme to hang on them.

Looking forward to the new version--this game deserves a deluxe copy! I have put the original up for trade since I have the new edition preordered.

RATING: 9.3
This is truly an amazing game!

The widespread word is that this is a heavy and hard game, but this judgment must be due to some difficulty interpreting the not so clear rules. I really didn't notice this because the game was shown to me by a player mate and seemed pretty straightforward. In fact, later in the game, was possible to understand how several features combine to work in such an ingenious way.
The game has a little hand managment that is no more than an excuse to push the game foward, but what is really important are the only two actions (per player) allowed on each turn...but there is so much to it!
At first, the resources are very sparse and the actions must be planned and optimised (a bit of AP must be allowed) but later, near the end of the first phase of the game, the buildings and connections created on the board start to work in full swing, selling production to the markets in automatic mode, is like the board suddendly came to life before our eyes and one feel's like he was really creating a new industrial country...brilliant!

RATING: 10
While I didn't much care for this game on my first play through, I have really come around after giving it another chance.  This is not AoS, or even close.  The interplay/interaction between the players has a unique feel that takes getting used to.  There is an element of cooperation for mutual benefit, but it does not have to be played that way.  It's hard to be cutthroat, since snaking someone's move likely helps them a bit anyway.  However, the play does allow for different styles, which I really like.  It takes some time to make the adjustments that really make this game enjoyable, so give it another chance if you didn't care for it on the first try.

RATING: 2
After a couple plays, not impressed. I'm not really sure how much variation there's going to be. It sure seems like building in the center, both in terms of buildings and canals/rails is the way to go, and that building ships should only be done when you're out of options. This is the strategy I used in my second (and hopefully last) game and won handily.

Frankly, this is the worst kind of euro. This game epitomizes the "spreadsheet as a game" criticism that so many euros have come under. It's a bean counting exercise with no interaction between the players. At least in most euros you can use denial as a tactic or form long term strategies. That doesn't even exist here. To add insult to injury the theme is thinly pasted on, and there's unnecessary chrome for one of the locations.

RATING: 9
Players are competing to manufacture and transport goods, across the canals and railways of Lancashire, during the industrial revolution.

Initial reaction: Good God, man, these rules are obtuse!
Reaction after one play: Good God, man, this game is great!

Yes, there are a lot of exceptions in the rules. Yes, you'll be looking them up repeatedly, during your first game. Yes, you will make bad decisions during your first game. Yes, it is all worth it.

This is a meaty game, full of tough decisions, diverse strategies, varied paths to victory, and with very little luck involved. The competition isn't as fierce and tight as [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/game/4098]Age of Steam[/url], but it is more forgiving.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Economic money + victory points game with a multitude of ways to get both which all interact with each other via coal and iron resources.
There's the usual tension between pursuing money and when to switch to chasing VPs and all decisions give you a bit of both. It also has a very blatant up-front choice of whether to go heavily into debt to hopefully boost your earnings through the game or to eek out a path on minimal loans.
There is a fair bit of randomness on the card draws which then limit what you can do (unless paying 2 cards for a wild) which usually results in me playing tactically based on what's available now.
Works very well, plays reasonably fast with the right group, there still no obvious best path. I like it a lot.

RATING: 3.5
I've been wanting to learn Brass for years and years and years.  I finally got the digital game and I have to admit that I am quite disappointed.  I'm sure there is a good game in there, but the rules and theme are so randomly and poorly matched up that it's impossible to keep all the rules straight in my head. Especially when coupled with the fact that sometimes the theme makes sense and at other times is doesn't at all.

So at first glance (well after putting 3 or 4 hours into the game) I think this game falls into the category of games that rate highly on the BGG but I don't like.  Those where the complexity of rules seem to be half of what people are attracted to.  I like depth of game play, but I want it to be coupled with intuitive rules.

RATING: 8
Not sure if I should give it an 7 or an 8 rating? I assume I'll like it even more once I play it again so I'll go with 8 for now.

First of all, despite only 6 pages of actual rules this is not an easy game to learn, teach or play. After one game most things have cleared up but never play I first game without a good player aid, it will help you immensely.

I really liked the ebb and flow of teh game, you start out small and have to decide between building tiles that will go away soon or develop new better ones. Many hard choices. After half the game most of the game board is reset as the rail era begins. Now the game really become competitive and the right cards are crucial but perhaps too important, at least to us newbie players?

All in all a game well worth trying out and one I will return to, but be prepared that it's not as easy as it looks.

RATING: 9
Finicky and demanding, it is all about managing a hand of cards that give cruelly-limited options.  It is difficult to do exactly what you want simply with the cards you're given and the restrictions of the map and building rules.  Compound this by completing only a small piece of your plan each turn and 2-3 other players competing for the same spaces and the game quickly becomes difficult.  It is immensely satisfying to do well in this game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Update...After 5 plays over the years, I have to up my rating.  This is a really good game.  It is a brain burner that presents you with a lot of challenging decisions to make.  Do I build, what should I build, where can I build, should I invest, take a loan, build links, etc.   Bumping up from 7 to an 8...and maybe it should be a 9.

While not as good as the hype, I enjoyed playing.  You are placing tiles down based on what kind of cards you have in hand.  Then attempting turn them over either to score money & points.  I'm not sure why this game is called Brass.  There's no brass in this game.  Probably should have been called Iron or Coal.  It feels like the cotton mills are difficult to setup to use correctly. You need ports. If you don't draw any ports or cities w/ports, it is impossible to place them.  And if you are lucky enough to place ports, then someone else can wind up using your ports.  Which isn't bad since you get VPs, but you really want to recoup your cotton mill investment.  I'd like to play a bit more to test out some strategies, but at first glance, this one probably needed just a bit more development to be excellent.  Glad I played, but wouldn't buy it.

RATING: 7
While, I liked this game quite a bit at first, my opinion of it has dimmed over time. Most of the locals are less experienced in the game then me, and I tend to win a large percentage of my total games played. This does not bother me that much, as there are other games where this is also true, but unlike those I am unable to maintain interest and tension when playing with less experienced players. The game instead feels like I am simply going through the motions, performing obvious, easy actions that inevitably result in me winning. 

Now, I still enjoy this game quite a bit when playing with people who are experienced at the game, and I think it is a great game when playing with such people, but the only way I am going to be able to continue to do so is to play it on-line (which is not going to happen), or to play with the locals until they get better (unlikely to happen). So as it stands it is staying in my collection until enough time has passed that I feel like playing it again or I start playing it with more experienced players.

RATING: 7
extremely complex but filled with challenging strategizing. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Players are industrialists during the canal/early railway age in the United Kingdom.  Players take turns playing two cards to take two actions.  Actions can be industrialize (build stuff), build canals or railways, borrow money, sell cotton, or do a little R&D.  You're trying to boost your turn to turn income, and build long term victory points, by getting your industry utilized.  A fairly quick and breezy game (our first game took 90 minutes, four players) truly mired in a set of rules determined to make learning the game as difficult as possible.  Part of the problem is the four of five ways to score income/VP each has it's own set of rules, and they seldom intersect.  Bit worried that a screwy hand of cards screws you, too.  I can see this going the way of too many Warfrog games - nod admiringly at it, and never get it off the shelf again.  I did enjoy the game, but the investment in ensuring we play it correctly took around a week.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Well worth playing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Wonderful game. You have to find a balance of strategy, tactics, and opportunity in the hand you're dealt. The two actions you get per turn are rarely enough to complete the action you want, so gaming the turn order is vital to get turns when you want them.

There's tension and opportunity cost between all of your options: build coal/iron now and take an immediate payoff? build/ship cotton early for the foriegn market, or build up more cotton & ports to bulk ship later? build now, or develop and build more valuable industry next turn... if the space is still available? Burn an industry card that might give you options later, or use a city card that might be your only way into a city you don't expect to use? take loans at the end of the canal age or flip one more tile?

Definitely in my top five.

RATING: 7
I'm no Martin Wallace fanboy, but this is a nice mid-weight econ game.  The cards constrain how many actions you've got and where you can use them, but still give you a fair amount of flexibility.  There's a good degree of indirect interaction between the players, since you can affect the supply & demand of coal/iron, as well as use each other's ports.  The midgame shift, where all the canals & low-level buildings are wiped from the board, is kind of dramatic, but as long as you've played before or have a good idea of what's coming it's not going to really mess with your plans.

Anyway, the unchanging map would probably get boring if I played it a lot, but there's Age of Industry if that ever happens.  There's also some confusion inherent in the rules as you have to check every action to see if you're cheating or not.  (Let's see, I can build this railway here b/c I have a presence in this city, and the coal can come from this mine here over these links to the job site..)  I enjoy brass because it doesn't feel to me like a painful uphill battle to make money (as some games are), but you don't have tons of cash to throw around, either.  It's also constrained to a decent length rather than a huge slog.

RATING: 6.5
This is a good game, but not one I want to play regularly.  I say it's a good game because the mechanics and the interplay between players is solidly done.  Scoring is a bit fiddly, but otherwise, there's little to complain about with the game itself.  

It presents a fascinating puzzle of sorts as you need to think several turns ahead, but with a variable turn order.  Throw in a random card draw and you will literally feel your brain igniting as you try to suss out how best to use your resources while prepping contingencies in case some butthead decides to block a space.  It's a solid brain burner, for sure.

That being said, Brass is a game I can respect if not particularly enjoy.  While the strategic element is solid and engaging, I found the fun-factor to be absolute zero, and I've played heavier games than this one.  Something about it just felt like an extended academic exercise and less something that I would gather friends around to play.  Admittedly, these are my personal tastes, but I found that for what it accomplishes, similar results can be found in much more streamlined games that also include that fun-factor for me.

Would I play it again?  Sure.  It's a great game.  It's just not something I would pick up myself or suggest to bring to the table.

RATING: 10
Martin Wallace :star: :star: :star: :star: :star: 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A heavy euro with a unique theme.  In the game you will be building Cotton Mills, Ships, Ports, Coal Mines and Iron Works on the various cities on the board where they're allowed to be built.  You will also connect cities with canals or rails.  The objective is to get the most victory points.  VP are attained by having your tiles for the above mentioned industries used which cause them to be flipped and through the routes you have built.  The game takes place over a number of rounds in 2 phases.  Each round you and the other players will take turns discarding two cards form your hand to perform 1 - 2 actions.  These cards can somewhat dictate where or what type of industries can be built which adds an aspect to the hand management.  Between the 2 phases, some of the various industries and all routes will be removed from the game.  In addition, the second phase allows for building multiple industries in the same city by the same player.  The game really opens up and allows you to try and capitalize on the board situation.

There's a [b]lot[/b] more that can be said about the game but if you like heavy euros than this is part of the upper echelon and you would do well to give it a try.  Age of Industry has been released and shares much in common but it's been said that it is a lot easier to learn given that a lot of Brass's little niggly rules have been removed.  Some argue that with the rules that have been lobotomized, a lot of the game's character and charm was also was removed.  I haven't played Age of Industry so I can't really compare at the moment.  If I had to pick something about the game I didn't like, it would be that when the deck runs out and you only have 8 actions left for four  rounds the game really bogs down with people trying to optimize.

RATING: 4
Martin Wallace should go back to designing Waros with quirky themes, like Princes of the Renaissance and Byzantium. Those games work because they have a quirky theme and the typical Wallace quirks in the rules are accepted in such games for "flavour". But when he designs an out-and-out Euro game with no concern for theme, there are inevitable comparisons to other Euro games, and his games are just not as good. Brass is not the near-broken horror that Tempus was but it is still fiddly, non-intuitive and repetitive, and it has no theme. If I have to choose a card-driven area control game, I'd rather play Blue Moon City.

RATING: N/A
Heavy economic game from Wallace.  Second edition.  Wallace actually regards [gameid=39351] (slightly more mathy and economic than Brass) as one of his best.  See the [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/257485/brass-2-player-variant]2 player variant[/url].

The bottom line: Brass is not for the faint of heart, given that it requires overcoming the initial hurdle of a somewhat obtuse rulebook. It's not without its faults, and its threshold will simply prove too much for some gamers, especially those who find the rules too complicated, the length too long, and the gameplay too tough to get their head around. But those who enjoy this challenge and are prepared to overcome it will find one of the very best economic games of the modern era. It's a signature Martin Wallace design, where the narrative arc created by the rich historical theme is closely linked to the game's mechanics, and where all the elements come together to produce a very rich and deep experience that features challenging economics, tough decisions, diverse strategies, subtle player interaction, and almost unlimited replayability. If you consider yourself a serious gamer and enjoy economic games, Brass is a quintessential Wallace game absolutely not to be missed.

See my full review: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/819849

RATING: 8
This is one of those rare games that you not only enjoy, but you also appreciate and marvel at the way that it plays.  The board seems so simple.  The rules seem simple (at least for Martin Wallace).  But the game itself is fascinating to watch and play.  The supply and demand dynamics work beautifully.  Is it fun?  Yes, but cerebral fun.  After this your brain knows that it has had a workout and wants some crunchy light fillers to cool down.  One major gripe: the coins are horrible, cheap little plastic things.  They even feel weird.  Don't let them ruin the game.  Use high quality poker chips.  The game deserves it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Quite heavy on rules, sometimes the mechanics does not stick to the theme very well, or simply put they do not make much of a thematic sense (why steel can be "teleported" anywhere on the map? Steelworks should require a constant iron feed, don't they?!). Still, the mechanics ARE very interesting, and allow unfolding a satisfactory gameplay and rewarding strategic choices. It is deep, tense, and you do feel the sense of accomplishment throughout the game of building your own commercial network and buildings. Player interaction is quite high in a 4p game, and comes mostly from the area control (limited building spaces) / network control (canals and railways routes), partially from resources availability (carbon and iron), indirectly from using the resources / "activating" other player's buildings. I really like the components.. the map is beautiful, the chits comes out easily from cardstock and are durable, the cards are ok too. Still I would have preferred more graphical difference between front and back sides. The plastic money is awful (i.e. same as byzantium, coup). 

The re-playability is super-high due to the random draw of cards. After just one play I do have some concerns about the effective strategies that could be used which are either not well defined (which is a definite plus) or it is maybe more rewarding a balanced approach which eventually might flat-out the game a bit.

I would certainly advise this game to anyone in search for a good medium-heavy euro, with the big plus of an accomplishment feeling (agricola mastered that) that can appeal to more than one. Recommended.

RATING: N/A
