=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Top 5 game for me.  Desert island game.  I absolutely love this game despite its rough edges. I played about two dozen online games against some real Brass sharks and honed my skills as well as gained a great appreciation for this design. I'm over 30 plays and still captivated by this one. Sure, I'd love a new Brass map but there's tremendous replayability right here; there are always surprises in a Brass game for me.

The first two players should always develop as their action, but after those openings, the gambits begin -- tactical and strategic gambits in terms of going for immediate market-refilling coal/iron works vs taking the tempo in terms of cotton and/or building rail lines. There's a wonderful asymmetry here in terms of industries, and you'll need to pick a path soon enough. That path will inevitably intertwine you with other players, and you'll have to find ways to benefit from each other. I always tell players that you get 31 actions in the game, never more, maybe less (if you combine two into one). One of the fun challenges is using other players' stuff to your advantage. As I love games that rely on reading player incentives, this is heaven for me. When you're ahead of the game, you feel like the smartest guy in your gaming world; when it's the other way 'round, it's a bitter pill but makes you all the more determined to come back the next time. 

As to the game's weak points: the cards can feel like the wind behind you pushing you onward or in your face; the randomness of the foreign market can screw you; the rules are dense, and the Birkenhead exception is exceptionally fiddly. Still, I find it an engrossing play every single time because all those are true for everyone and you're all going to get slowed down in some way. Perhaps there's an odd play when one player benefits more than others, but griping about the cards is just an inexperienced player who hasn't figured out the creativity required to skin the cat a different way. Always have to leave yourself options, or better yet, plan out your turns in advance with the cards you have (admittedly easier done online than on table). 

In addition to different paths for scoring, there's a nasty defensive aspect to the game in hemming players in with their rail networks. I've had more than one game where I couldn't take an action on the last turn of the game b/c I let myself get fenced in. A lot of the game's strategy is predicated on the brilliant turn-order mechanism whereby the player who spent the most amount of money (i.e., you know, [i]did stuff[/i] goes last). So going last, spending the least, and then going first for two turns in a row is S.O.P. at least a couple times a game. Taking loans (described by one expert player as the pit stop of the Brass race) should be carefully planned as a refueling before getting back in the race. Every turn is so damn meaningful, and therein lies the brilliance of this game and its repeated allure. Can you coordinate your 31 better than the other guys?

Now, for me, it took several plays to get adept at it, but I stuck with it because something drew me on. I can easily see some players getting frustrated, blaming the cards and moving on. Alas for them. In summary, this game has a lot to teach a player if he commits himself to it, and rewards the player who's got an eye on what's going to happen next.

RATING: 10
Brass provides compelling progression.  Each turn you expand your empire in some fashion, but you have to balance short term rewards with long term investment and economic expansion.  Every game I've played has been tense, and played out vastly different than the previous.  On top of this, the rules are simple enough to grasp in a round or two of play.

That said, here are a few things that may not appeal to people:

Loans: you can reduce your income to take out loans.  A lot of the early game play revolves around this.  I find it tense and compelling, but others could find it obnoxious and frustrating.

Cards: you have to play to the hand you're dealt.  You can't decide "I'm going to go heavy ironworks this game!" You may not get those, and instead have a hand full of harbors.  My strategies change every time as a result.

Artwork: The game art is dull.  I am fine with that, but the muted palette will not appeal to everyone.

RATING: 9
Complex, deep, and lots of fun interaction.  It feels like a stand-alone game that brings a unique and fulfilling experience.  I'm still scratching my head on what exactly should be done to help increase my chances for victory.

Like most other Wallace games, there is one clunky element (virtual link) that makes the game slightly "fiddley".  It's a very minor issue - and not near as offensive as others (i.e., the external investment phase in Tinners' Trail).

There are two time periods - each with a slightly different "flavor" of gameplay.  Like Amun Re, it allows certain built items to be carried over to the next "era".

A fresh, unconventional, and probably one of Martin Wallace's very best designs.  Grade: A

RATING: 8.5
Like many other Martin Wallace games, Brass feels like a boardgame and history documentary rolled into one. Its mechanics are tightly coupled with its setting, and the game never feels dry. There is an interesting mixture of mechanics that includes route building, engine building, area control, card playing, and even some push-your-luck elements. As a result, there is never a shortage of interesting and difficult decisions to make.

Oddly, even though player turns involve the playing of cards, and I do enjoy card-driven games, the cards feel underutilized in Brass, serving mostly to determine where and what you can build, rather than introducing unique interactions into the game. However, Brass probably didn't need the extra complexity anyway, given the depth that its existing mechanics already provide.

This highly-strategic game feels unique amongst all of the other games in my collection, not because it introduces any single unique mechanic, but because of its special blend of familiar ones: hand management, track laying, developing technology, laying tiles (buildings) for possible victory points, managing actions and limited money to gain victory points,  There are a few paths to victory, and a lot to think about on and between every turn.  The rules take a while to understand and digest (and the rulebook could have been laid out better), but if you're not afraid of heavy games, this one is one of the best!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
EG Deluxe Edition. Nice and shiny well crafted very heavy good quality metal coins! Also great to have the 2 players map and instructions included! 

I've waited for this game a lot of time. Now I finally get it! My preciousss! No matter how many new board games and board game designers have appeared since Brass first edition: I think this is a  pure gem, a truly masterpiece of game design, and I consider its author, Martin Wallace as one of the best board game designers of all time.
To me, the merit of this game is that it achieves something that is very difficult to do in the boardgaming world: provide a very deep and varied game experience with a very simple set of rules. Here the complexity of gameplay comes from the richness and the variety of decisions players can make, not from an excessive amount of rules, rule exceptions, rule clarifications, etc. 
This, for sure, isn't a game for everybody, nevertheless: is there a game capable to satisfy all gamers, all gaming preferences? I'm pretty sure there's not such thing. Brass is certainly an economic game, in other words, its a game about developing an economic engine in the best possible way to get the most benefit from it. It's a game where is technically impossible for  a player to win by the means of luck, because there are almost no luck elements involved (well, the cards, but a that's a very manageable amount of luck, imo). 

So, not for casual, family kind-of gamers. This one,  seriously speaking, is a gamers game and, in my opinion, one of the bests out there. An authentic beast, a wonderful and profound game experience... What else could I say? I love this one badly, badly!

RATING: 7
What a complex little beast this thing is. It's not exactly rules intensive, although there are numerous conditional events. Essentially, we are paying to place various economy simulations on the board of coal, iron, textile, and shipping all flavored on the revolution of canal shipping into rail shipping in England. It is heavily abstracted, but the theme is there if you think about what is going on enough. 

The scoring and the discarding are both key game mechanics that took me most of the game to understand, and I still don't think I have it down. Long term planning in the game is possible, but it requires very careful attention to what cards you discard to use your action. I was far too casual about that aspect and missed its importance.

RATING: 3
Brass offered a very middle of the road  feel after my first play.  There seems to be a lot of rules, and exceptions built into what is essentially a very straight forward tactical game.  The cards limiting actions seems a little wonky.  It builds upon that constrained feel that limited actions should do alone without benefiting the game play.  In fact it feels as if it makes the game a little too dependent on timely draws to pursue any viable strategy.  In lighter games I like this mechanic of having to manage your hand (Masons, Oregon), but in Brass it just forces the feel that luck may eventually be more of a determinate for winning as anything else.  The ability and flexibility in and of the cards themselves doesn't offer enough variety to allow for the same sort of dynamic clever play as it does in such games as those mentioned above.  I definitely would play this again to iron out my opinion, but from an initial glance it doesn't seem to be one of Wallace's better designs.  And when it comes down to the bottom line this really has a poor time to weight ratio.

RATING: 7
Thought it would be a lot harder to explain the rules, but it only took about 10 minutes.  The problem isn't that the game is hard to understand, but it's very hard to figure out what you need to do to stay afloat, if that makes any sense.  The first half dozen turns were a constant struggle to balance your money with what resources other players might want to use.

It's been a long time since I've wanted - NEEDED! - to play a game again after the first run-through.

After a second game, I really understand it now.  I'm still no good, but I enjoy the long-term planning required to play this game well.

RATING: 3
Extremely dry.  Do not try this with anyone other than hardcore gamers.  This is one of those "It's rated high because hardcore Euro gamers dominate BGG ratings" games.

Played and didn't like it even though I did reasonably well more by happenstance than skill.

Seems like a game that you need to play a half-dozen times to really figure out relative values and strategies.

Since my one play was not very enjoyable, I won't be investing any more time in this.

Update: Gaming group played it again a year or two later, so I had to join in. Again, I tied for highest VP, simply pursuing a strategy no one else was (as works in most Euro games)  Very samey to other economic Euros. Manipulate cards, $ vs VP, connection elements to maximize VPs. Still dry. Still a few too many mechanisms thrown together. That I did so well without knowing all the efficiencies or gameflow makes me feel it's not as deep as others make it out to be.

RATING: 7.5
I bought this from Martin Wallace because I've played and enjoyed previous titles of his. It seemed my type of game (Indonesia, Caylus, PR) and it was.
It is a weighty game, complex enough rules that take quite a bit of getting around for some people. But I think the mechanics in the game work really well together and once you balance out the actions and your options you can really get into the swing of it. What is really good about it as well is that there is no set strategy that you really should use. It is very opportunistic on your turn.

RATING: 8
An excellent game. The economy is very subtly balanced so that development and loan actions do pay off by the end of the game so long as you use them wisely. With four players the game is very tight, and you can need to be very careful to ensure the cards don't upset your game too much; with three, the game is easier but then you get more actions and therefore more time and space to really get your economy going.
While there are a fair number of fiddly rules, I find that it's easy enough to remember them based on the underlying theme.
All in all, definitely one of my favourites!

RATING: 9
Martin Wallace has some dedicated fans.  For me, my appreciation started off slow.  I didn't enjoy Age of Steam, or Tempus, or La Strada.  Then, I played Liberte and thought it was great.  Brass is also a great game.  It doesn't feel as heavy as other titles, maybe because it is more forgiving?  It's very dynamic in balancing production, flipping tiles, but then staying on top of upgrading, but at the cost of income, and... well, that sounds a little dull doesn't it?  You really feel like you are building your industries and you have several ways to grow and several things you can concentrate on.  At the same time, the game is fun and doesn't feel like a math optimization exercise.

The components are top notch and this is definitely the best looking title from Warfrog as well.  I'm looking forward to playing this again.

RATING: 9
[b]Brass[/b] [i]Martin Wallace 2007[/i] 
Brass seems to be at first sight an economical game, and normally, I dont like games of this sort, but Brass is just an amazing eurogame designed for the euro geeks and not for the casual gamers. However it is not really dificult to teach (although the rules are somewhat fiddly and easy to forget) but it is normal that in their first games the players have no clue about the best options to take, and usually they feel overwhelmed by the game. Just try it again, you could end loving it as I did. In my book, this the best of the Martin Wallace´s designs and this is saying a lot. An excellent game that works perfectly with three to four players and has also a very good variant for two players sessions.

RATING: 10
An exquisitely balanced and deeply strategic network and economy building game. High-level play is a finely-tuned dance of "coopetition". The only downside is that the ruleset is extremely complex, full of little easy-to-forget points, with the game becoming utterly broken by omitting or misplaying even the smallest and fiddliest of little rules. Once all players understand the entire ruleset, though, it is almost unmatched among strategy games. Wallace's masterpiece.

RATING: 10
Excellent game. Very strategic, yet thematic as well. So much to think about and prepare for next rounds and period. In first period, canal period, you mostly try to earn and develop as efficient you can and prepare for next period, rail period, when you will have really tough decisions, what to build and where to do that. Much player interaction on map. The only drawback is that cards add some restrictions where to build, but that is very minor. You just have to use your cards as good as you can. Also I recommend to print out some custom player aids.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I'll caution that I've played just once.  I suspect there's a good game to be found here, but I also suspect it may not be worth the search.  The rules are horribly presented and the goals are opaque and mysterious, at least initially.  I'm confident that things would become clearer with further play, but I'm much less confident that the game provides a tight enough focus for the players.  There's a lot of opportunistic nickle and dime-ing going on, spending time maneuvering around the inscrutable restrictions placed on you by the game system and by your hand of cards.  This maneuvering was kind of a pain in the ass, and nothing I was doing in the game was exciting enough to make the effort pay off.

I think the bottom line on Brass is the same complaint that I've had with pretty much all of Martin Wallace's post-Age of Steam Warfrog games:  too many rules.

And it's not just the quantity of rules that I question. It's the artificial and unexplained nature of many of the rules. While playing, I kept asking myself the questions, "Why is this rule the way it is? Why is this game the way it is?" Why do I need a card to play here? Why does my location card work here but not my industry card? Why is this shipyard worth victory points but not income? Why is this cotton mill worth income but not victory points? Why is my infrastructure suddenly disappearing? Why must I use coal from this source? Why do I want to build my coal mine after people have been using coal from the general supply, but not before? Why is my turn order determined by the amount I spent last turn? Why does my income not count for anything at the end of the game? Why is it so easy for me to take out monstrous loans with almost no penalty? Why does it matter whether a port is flipped or unflipped for some things but not for others? What does that represent? Why do I have to annoy myself and choose a card to discard even when my action does not require a card? Why am I able to draw cards for a location that is inaccessible during this phase of the game?

I guess the point I'm trying to make is that the more ambitious and complex a game is, the more intuitive and coherent its rules and theming need to be. Otherwise, players will have trouble organizing and making sense of all the information. It's a very bad sign when the rule book includes an apology at the end saying something like, "There really is no good way to explain this game."  Wow.

RATING: 5
I am not sure why I like this one, it is a little more EURO than my typical favorites. Maybe it is because it is not long like Agricola or Puerto Rico and I still haven't got the strategy down to win yet, so it's very challenging. 
I don't like the mechanic that lets you burn through technologies very much, but I do admit it works well somehow. 
This is very much a game of looking at what your opponents have set you up to do and maximizing your effectiveness based on that, while trying not to leave them set up for a huge play - yet it is not nearly so punishing in that respect as Puerto Rico. I also like being able to take loans without feeling like I am digging my own grave like in some games.

RATING: 7
Another ingenious masterwork from Martin Wallace, and a worthy companion design to Age of Steam. It feels a bit less cutthroat than Age of Steam, and more forgiving, too, as your mistakes are paid for with opportunity costs rather than the bankruptcy cash spiral of that other game. Handsome components. The theme is brilliantly realized. From other comments I thought the components would be ugly, the rules a mess, and he card mechanic too random, but found none of these to be the case. The rules are a bit gritty and might have benefited from more development, but there's nothing you can't get down pat here after a single learning game and a post-contest review of the rules.

RATING: 5
I liked the game, but I can't say I'm jonesing to play it again.  It could go up with another play as the game has a lot of potential.  I need to try it again to give it a fair shake.  It had a lot to like, but it seemed really dry and robotic.  I really disliked the card mechanic in the game.  The cards are flexible enough that the only time they matter is when they screw you over - I would have rather seen a more typical turn/timing mechanism and the elimination of the cards.  A lot of the odd little rules seemed to add fiddliness without adding additional interesting decision points.  The technological development part of the game is pretty cool, as is the chrome between the canal phase and the rail phase.  This is another game where every turn you are trying to plan what you are going to do, and you nearly always seem to come up a couple of dollars short or get screwed by another player's actions, so you always need a backup plan.  The boat VPS are huge, but our game was won by players who instead worked on building networks of lesser-scoring buildings and railways.  The game was way too long for what it was.  Why should this take 3 hours?  It could be done in 2 or less and be just as much fun.  We also didn't see any kingmaking, but like many martin wallace designs, it looks to me like one player's mistake might be another player's bounty.  A couple crap moves by one player taken advantage of by the other player could decide the whole thing.  A little arbitrary for a 3 hour game.  The components were unimpressive and overall the game didn't feel like it really had any "take that" element to it, even though we were interacting on the board regularly.  I just didn't have much fun.  An OK Game.  I'll adjust my rating if/when I play it again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Within a couple of plays, I realized that this is going to be a classic, and a favourite of mine for a long time.  Great replayability and lots of depth.  The only things against it are that you can only play with 3 or 4 players (no 2- or 5-player variants in the official rules at least), and it's hard to explain the game to non-gamers.  But maybe I should score it a 10/10 anyhow.

The hints in the (2nd edition?) rule book are exactly right -- this is a game of supply and demand.  Try to build what other players need.  If there are a lot of cotton mills, build ports, and vice versa.  If there is little coal or iron in the market, build some (and get the monetary bonus for fulfilling the demand).

Money is not as tight as in Age of Steam -- don't be afraid of taking loans to get your economic engine running.

It seems critical to place railroads around big cities (e.g. Manchester) that are likely to fill up with buildings that will get turned over.  This may require taking loans (many loans?) at the right time to allow you to build 2 rails for $15, or maybe 4 rails for $30 in a turn!  For building rails, it's also important to get to go first (if possible) in the rail phase.  In fact, I've seen people choose "loan, loan" as their last two actions in the canal phase to ensure that they have $60 and go first.  Of course, getting enough coal may be a problem if you want to build 4 rails, especially because you need to connect to a port in order to buy from the free market.  If you build coal at the end of the canal phase, other players may use it instead of you... so put it somewhere obscure (like Rochdale).

It's probably too hard to get a shipyard down for only 18 VPs.  Try to get ironworks down instead -- the best one pays out 9 VPs, plus a little income.  And it stops other people from playing ironworks (because there are so few spots on the board for them).  Of course, playing them too early can be a problem -- you could be overbuilt if there is no more iron left on the board.

Useful tip:  develop (discard out of the game) some 1-cost tiles early, and play 2-cost tiles in the canal phase.  This gives you bases to start from in the rail phase, and points that can score twice (once after each phase).

RATING: 9
Very interesting connection/economy-building game. However, my first play was after playing a few other games and many players at the table were pretty burnt out, so the game was long, drawn out, and involved a lot of questions (and arguments) about rules.

I can see potential here however. For a game that is very much about developing an economy and then developing a point strategy (e.g., Saint Petersburg), the spatiality of it all, the limitations that your cards place on you, and
the interaction that occurs through others using your buildings and connections make this game much more complex and rich web of interaction. However, this can become paralyzing for folks who have a difficult time formulating distinct, differentiated, and definitely fluid short term and long term goals.

I would gladly play again.
-----------------------------------------------------------
Upate (07/09/2014): After playing a second time, I'm appreciating the game all the more. I still feel as though there are still oh so many exceptions to the rules (e.g., what buildings can and can't be overbuilt, and the conditions under which you can), but I also recognize that the game is designed to only crumble under the most serious of analysis paralysis (as you *only* take up to 2 actions per turn). Furthermore, as I chased an plows-into-plowshares strategy this game, I recognize the ways in which pursuing this 'basic' and typical Euro strategy begins to break down and break down relatively quickly under players who effectively, and frequently draw loans but who stay afloat as well. Ranking 6-->7. Likely to continue climbing.

----------------------------------------------------------
Update September 2015:
There are far more opportunities to cut people in/out of entire economic sectors than I thought (it really is far more than a strict economic game). The tempo is delicious as players jockey and jostle to have the ol' one-two punch (or at least landing that second punch before a player who is encroaching on the same turf reals from the first one). The economic 'band' helps prevent anyone from running away with a lot of cash, keeps the focus on points (across both ages especially) and makes loans a dance rather than a dread. This game goes from good to great with a vicious table.

RATING: 9
Very good game. I actually bought this game after having played Age of Industry, which succeeded Brass as a game with same mechanisms and more streamlined rules.

Comparing Age of Industry and Brass, I really think Brass is the better game. The game has been criticized for it's fiddly rules. If you are used to playing some of the typical Euros (e.g. Puerto Rico) you really shouldn't worry- rules are actually very simple.

What I really like about this game is tha balance between short time gain and long term strategy, the fight for the board positions, the supply/demand mechanisms for coal and iron and the interaction with other players. Money is tight, and you need to balance building a certain cash flow and long term strategies. Excellent game - recommended for anybody who enjoys strategic games with some depth. Replay value is very high, as there are several possible winning strategies, leaving you with a desire to try something new next time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I'm glad someone taught this game. I heard the rules were poor, so I was reluctant to get this despite the high ratings. The game mechanics are relatively simple: 1) play two cards--one per action; and when everyone is done, pick up cards and income. Play until all the cards are gone. Brilliant. Yet, the "persistence" rules, building, taking of coal, building canals and tracks, taking of iron, all make for a clumsy game that clocked at 4.5 hours for a game of 4, with 3 newbies. It has a similar feel to Power Grid, Caylus, and the various railroad games that Wallace is famous for. The complexity does not come from the mechanics, but rather the strategy. It took half the game to get a hold of what was going on--it all makes sense. Build coal mines to supply coal for building advanced buildings and tracks. Build iron works to supply iron for development (to race past initial buildings to "choice" buildings with more VPs). Build ports to ship the cotton. Build cotton mills to produce the cotton. If deliveries are established for coal, iron, and cotton, the income goes up (like Railroad Tycoon). Loans up to 30 pounds can be taken as an action, but knock you back accordingly. There are two phases: the canal phase (which is painfully slow), and the rail phase (which is much quicker). Only advanced buildings built during the first phase survive into the second phase, continuing to give VPs. The more advanced the building, including ships, the less income it brings in, but gives correspondingly higher VPs. Income is separate from VPs, although they share the same track (this is a departure from RRT), and VPs are scored only at the end of the two phases. All in all, a complex game, comprising of deceptively simple mechanics. There is a lot of depth here, and I am looking forward to exploring more of that. Initial rating: '8'.

Update (3/29/09): After playing Brass and Le Havre, it seems that Brass makes more sense to me. Le Havre seems like I am playing Agricola, and Agricola currently aggravates me. Brass is more like Power Grid--it just makes more "sense" to me.

Update (08/06/10): Finally got my own copy, sensing that it is about to go OOP, in favor of Age of Industry. This is a fantastic game, although it has a pretty high learning curve in terms of mechanics and strategy, but it is worth it for those looking for a meaty tactical and strategic game.

Update (09/16/10) 5 plays: I keep losing, but keep coming back for more. The game makes me ponder about what mistakes I made, as well as the killer moves I did make. Toying with the Distant Market is a crap shoot, but fills me with glee if I make it. The game is tense, and it is impossible to come up with a plan and stick with it, because other players are always getting in the way, and the cards drawn give new opportunities. There is so much to do, yet so little time to do it in, plus everyone is competing for initiative/turn order, location, and opportunity. There is also plenty of interaction in terms of scoring, as sometimes it is worthwhile, or necessary, to flip over an opponent's industry tile, just to get the iron/coal cheap, or to use the port to ship the cotton. The emotions run high: the highs are exhilarating AND the lows are devastating. This manic-depressive experience keeps me coming back; it is addictive, and it is that good.

Update (11/05/10) 9 plays: Finally winning with cotton mill strategy, but it depends on what everyone else is doing and responding to their actions. One cannot play Brass in a vacuum--the game has one of the highest indirect player interactions I've ever played, since everyone is competing for finite building spaces and limited resources--someone is always getting in the way of another! However, player order manipulation, money management, opportunistic play, adaptability, and long-term planning are key. There is so much meaty play here, sprinkled with a bit of luck of the draw, that this must be board gaming heaven. I am upping this to a '10'.

Update (11/19/10) 12 plays: Definitely requires considerable hand-holding to get newbies past the first game, as the experience will probably make or break it for them. Reading the background in the rulebook was helpful for me to fully understand the intent behind the seemingly bizarre mechanics and rules. Explaining it in the following manner may be helpful in providing context to newbies:

* Coal is straightforward in terms of logistics, as you must have a traceable path back to a source, whether it be a coal mine or a port to access the market. Iron is NOT really the metal, but abstractly represents ideas, technology, progress, or sweat equity, and that is why it is immediately available everywhere on the board. Consider the box cover: there is a man holding up a miniature iron works model, which could conceivably represent the entrepreneurial spirit, and hence iron works are more like "think tanks" rather than something to do with the metal.

* Canals and Level 1 buildings don't really disappear at the end of the canal phase, as their productivity is captured in higher residual income for the rest of the game. Overbuilding is the same as well, as income either remains the same or increases; only the "prestige" or "influence" of the technologically superior industry is represented by the swing in VP.

* Flipping cotton mills and port tiles is equivalent to making cube deliveries in Railroad Tycoon or AOS, as you are not really delivering one cube, but rather establishing a trade route (a supply and demand relationship) that will pay residual income for the rest of the game. The same can be said of coal mines, iron works, and shipyards, as flipping represents maturation of a given industry, and thus comes with residual income.

* The random cards represent business opportunities that pop up all around the board. So, do you have presence or influence in that town for a new startup? Can you get the coal there? How much is it for the next level of technology?

* Loans are somewhat counterintuitive, as you are not paying fixed interest in the traditional sense every turn for some loan amount, but instead you are giving up residual income (and future earnings) as a sort of collateral. It may be helpful to consider loans as capitalization, much like issuing stock, or maybe like a reverse mortgage. Thus, coal mines are actually helpful, because although they provide poor victory points, they have high income, which can later be painlessly converted into loans that allow you to build more valuable industries.

Update (09/17/11): Played a custom Catalan variant, which feels and plays completely differently from the Lancashire board. Highly recommended.

Update (03/09/12) 35 plays: Hadn't played this in nearly five months, but it is still a winner. It is still a '10'.

Update (02/10/13) 43 plays: Let's not beat around the bush: this is a GREAT game from a GREAT designer. I'm now intrigued about what the designer did to make Age of Industry so much more "accessible".

Update (10/18/2013) 46 plays: Played a fan-based variant of French Brass. Different geography, different feel, but still a wonderful experience. Two shipyards allowed during the Canal phase? Count me in!

Update (11/15/13) 48 plays: Put together the PnP [thing=138329]Catalan variant[/thing] and played it. All four of us had forgotten that coal is extremely scarce. The board is evil. Forget all the strategies you've learned on the Lancashire board, because it isn't going to work. Brutal. Loved it. Will start separately recording plays there.

RATING: 8
[imageid=1671102 original]
[size=7]Fig. 1 — [i]At Last! A Contrivance to Exorcize the Devil from Idle Hands[/i][/size]

[i]"Gentlemen!  We have discovered the cure for indolence!  Through force of will—and a not inconsiderable amount of Capital—we shall transform the indigent and their natural lassitude into a veritable hive of industrious bees.  Upon their backs we shall become eaters of honey!  And then later we'll use steam engines to make giant robots, or something."[/i]

— The Inestimable Percy Brassacks, Esq.
Vice-Consul, [i]His Majesty's Committee on the Question of the Impecunious[/i], 1789

Finally, a game where orphans can dig coal, shovel whatnot into foundries, and spin cotton as Nature intended!  Much like Mr. Brassack's "beehive", the world of the past will crawl into the future on a moving carpet of the unfortunate.  Pick a better uterus next time, kids!

Initial contact was rough, as this comes across as a bog-standard Euro where one grinds nibs to trade for nobs and score dinguses; it was with great joy that I discovered this is really a storytelling game ([i]my favorite kind[/i]), the story of the sprouting and flourishing of the Industrial Revolution in Great Britain.  All those little rules, those teeny-tiny rules everyone forgets to hate, make no sense when taken at face value...  They are chrome, or, if you will, the lint and coal dust that gilds the dim windows of the noisome factories.  Without them you could forget that each Pound Sterling you plunk down lands squarely on someone's bare foot.

I want to play this one to death, to learn its secrets, to see into those dark depths where we trade cash for self-respect.  I [i]love[/i] it.

[i]"We call the li'l one 'Niney', what that 'e's only got nine fingers.  Well, most of 'em do—but that one there's ol' Niney anyways."[/i]

Évekig vártam arra, hogy játszhassak ezzel a híres játékkal. Talán a túlzott elvárások miatt, de nem hozott olyan élményt, mint amilyenre vártam. Nagyon gondolkodtató játék, ami nem lenne baj, de nagyon száraz a játék. Nagyon hosszú minden része, az előkészület, az akciók, az elpakolás, a pontozás minden hosszú, nehézkes. Simán előfordulhat, hogy egy adott körben első vagyok, de sokat költök azért a következő körben utolsó leszek, így pedig rengeteget kell várnom két lépés között. 
A szabály bonyolult, sok a kivétel és külön szabály, ráadásul nem mindegyiket érzem logikusnak. Ahhoz képest, hogy milyen komoly játék, nagy szerepe van egy jókor felhúzott város kártyának, és hiába lehet két kártyával kiváltani, mert akkor meg az elmaradt második akció hiányzik.

RATING: 9.88
If ever I had someone I wanted to show what board games can [i]do[/i], I’d choose Brass. It takes familiar elements—income, loans, route-building, resource economy—and distills them into a game whose like I don’t expect to see again. It’s not your daddy’s engine-building game; it’s not your grandpa’s city-building game. Its tech tree drives the economy as well as everyone’s on-board development, built networks, and scoring. Every element of this game is tied tightly to every other. You can’t build a link or develop an industry or commit it to production without shaking the balance of the overall board.

And yet there’s room for incredibly creative play. Because every part of the game is part of the engine—because the game [i]is[/i] an engine—you can try to monopolize any part of it. You can try to develop your ships and piggyback in some ore refining; you can try to industrialize all the ports yourself and let the money roll in while you build links connecting everything you can. You can ship, ship, ship cotton until foreign demand is all dried up and you start committing your own ports to British distribution. You can play Brass how you like, and you and everyone else will have to react to how everyone else and you are inserting yourselves into the engine. The engine works fine; but players may have to compensate like mad to deal with what they themselves are doing to it.

At its heart, Brass, the engine-building game, is a game about logistics. You get no engine if you can’t get your logistics running. You have to plug in where you can plug in and start operating from there. You have to reach out and work with what’s around you, because getting an in for your engine defines where your little part of the engine fits in the big one, and that governs your strategy in every aspect.[color=#ffffff]a9Z4MnAx[/color]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.8
1.三名玩家：，先分發手排，運河時期移除 9 張，鐵路時期移除 6 張。
四名玩家：先分發手牌兩張，運河時期移除 6 張，鐵路時期移除 2 張。
2.使用卡片兩張(卡片可以兩張當認亦地點使用)
可做以下行動:
A.建設工廠
(1)地點卡可以在當地建設，工業卡則需在運河/鐵路直接連結處（鐵路時期尚可以重覆蓋在一個區域，運河時期一個區域只有一棟）。
(2)若需要煤礦，有煤礦廠與海外市場兩種方法。
前者需有直接連結的煤礦廠，且先從最近的煤礦廠取得。
若場上沒有煤礦場，或者無運河/鐵路連到，可以採後者連結至已建設港口購買 (包括外部港口以及不論有無翻面且已蓋上港口指示物的地區，ex Livepool），但是必須有運河/鐵路連結到已建設的港口。
海外價格依造需求表支付，若無資源時隨時皆可用 5 元購買。
若煤礦廠與鐵礦場之資源取盡，可以翻面並更改收入格數，移動的格數等於圓圈內的數字。
(3)若是需要鐵礦，只要場上有資源則必須直接取用，無距離的限制。沒有取用則從海外購買。
(4)需能支付建設費用；若需使用需求欄的資源方塊，費用尚包括之，
並將總使用經費擺至花費總額。
(5)蓋完煤礦場與鐵礦場，依據左上生產資源。
若海外需求表有缺乏，且該煤礦有連結到已建設港口
（如前面 1 所示)，即刻賣出並填滿需求表，並依據價格收取現金。
(6)你可以無條件用同種且等級高的工業標示物蓋過自己的建物。
你也可以用同種且等級高的工業標示物蓋過他人的工廠(煤、鐵)，
前提是需求欄與版圖上已無該資源。
(7)限制條件：必須等極低的先建、不能建設等級為零的建築物、建築物標示物有運河/鐵路的圖案只
能在運河/鐵路時期建造、等級 1 的工業只能在運河時期蓋，鐵路時期只能蓋等級 2 以上的工業。
(8)特殊規定：利物浦與伯肯黑德之間有虛擬連結，無法運煤礦與棉花。
B.建設運河鐵路: 需要與自己的工業建設相接或者運河。
C.研發:需要鐵來研發1~2個。
D.賣棉:賣給正面朝上的港口與遠方市場，前提是必須直接相連。若使用遠方市場，先進行翻
面判定。只要還有市場需求，增加的收入等級為棉花廠的圓圈收入加上需求欄旁邊的數字。若市場沒有需求，則無法翻面，且此次行動立即結束。棉花廠與港口/海外市場可以連翻多組，需事前指定兩兩一組為何。棉花廠一定得自己的，港口則不一定。
計分 :
 1.已翻面的工業標示物的六角型內數字。
 2.運河/鐵路連接兩端的金色圓圈數，
(每一翻面的工業標示物皆有一個，境外地區與三個特殊地區都有兩個金色圓圈。)
 3.鐵路時期結束每 10 元有 1 分。

RATING: 7
Very much a diamond in the rough. It's an economic development game needing lots of fiddly restrictions in a heavy rulebook that's difficult to reference. I like the way each industry has its own set of characteristics and there's some pretty serious hand management involved with the cards, but the numerous sub-systems feel clunky and disjointed, there's none of the elegance of Steam or even Last Train to Wensleydale. Above all though, there's a higher random factor in which cards you get and how quickly external markets deteriorate than I want to see in a game of this length. A quirkily attractive game, one I've learned to love, but in design terms not the best of Wallace.

RATING: 9
A great economics game that is difficult to describe but easy to play.  After a round or two, it all comes together.

The economy is tight and you have to plan what cards to play and which to hold, but there's enough slack in the system to allow you to recover from the occasional mistake.  There are tough decisions to make and a lot of strategies to try.

As a sign of a great game, you're usually 1 coin short of being able to follow your perfect strategy - and all because you bought something last turn when you could have waited.

Everything you do affects others, but there's no direct competition, but you'll find yourself stymied at every turn as your opponent's build a canal just where you didn't want it, or take the last cotton mill in Colne.

Easily one of Martin Wallace's best games.

Just for hardcore gamers. It has a extremly high simulation level of the economic and industrial development. Martin Wallace has engaged different mechanisms (hand managment, resource managment, logistics and a bit of card driving games) and he designed a very detailed game. RATING: 8.5
I've only played two games; actually I am still in the middle of the 2nd game, but I am very immersed in the play. Though the number of rules might seem initially off-putting and the opening moves quite open, the game play settles into a very pleasant rhythm with consistently interesting choices, a clear sense of "if I only had one more action", and a compelling need to consider the play of others.
Wallace does an excellent job, given the limits of a board game, of mimicking the individual competitive side and the cooperative side of the rise of industry. Set-up time and put away time remains a little problematic, but I expect I will find some good ideas on how to be more efficient in the files as well as some more streamlined rules play. The chart and the frequently forgotten rules box included in the rules is a smart step in that direction by the makers themselves.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Two plays with 3 and 4 people. Brass shares some of its mechanics and part of its theme with Age of Steam, but it is a more streamlined game than AoS. The pricing is relatively straightforward, the difference between income and cash is clear and the only difficult part of the rules (where and under what circumstances you can build industries) becomes second nature before the end of the first game. The subtle competition (and, sometimes, cooperation) between players and the way that the game simulates a market for coal, iron and cloth really give Brass an authentic feel. The cards are a nice addition to a Martin Wallace game and, for once, the designer has resisted the temptation to throw in a die or two.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Network connections:economic:hand management:civ-like?  

Holy Moly! Best eat your wheaties before your first game or two of this whopper. Martin has graced us with another beaut of a Gamers game here, a VERY involved and engrossing game, thanks Martin!

Almost on par with POTR and AOS for the actual meat on the bone, very close.  While there are essentially only two ways to score once I heard the rest of the rules I was drowning in the lovely details to watch for.  This is a game of building and upgrading as efficiently as possible, in the right locations while also trying to connect to as many lucrative locations as possible, you are building for income and also for points.  Sounds easy but trust me it is not and will be very tricky with 4 players, I think 3 is a little more unforgiving so it was good number for the first learning game of this.  

Rules were a little tricky and took the best part of a game to really master, strategy was also somewhat elusive for the first half of my first game.  Lots of planning required, definitely a high strategy game and so far it appears to have some somewhat different approaches.  Also gamers will be happy to know there is very little luck in this game, it is card driven however the cards are multipurpose and you get to hold 8 in your hand while only having to play 2 per turn, this really minimizes the luck of the draw.  Component wise nice to see full-size cards but they were too thin, money is that plastic crappy money like AOS and other than that everything else was nice and up to par. 

The board will always have the same outline like RRT or AOS, this coupled with almost no random elements could hurt replayability/freshness a little unless a new map is introduced ala AOS however the card draw will help this somewhat as will your own different approaches to how you play a given game.  Overall I feel another very good game I'm looking forward to playing more, games should get down to 2 hours after a few plays, until then expect to see 2.5 to 3 depending on whether you have 3 or 4 players. 

[u]UPDATE:[/u] Well there might be a little more luck here than I first thought. The cotton shipping really is a little silly with a -4 stuck in the pile, totally reminds me of the random Indians while settling in AOEIII which I also didn't like. While thematic I wish these kind of elements were left out of long high strategy games, at least here you can hopefully build your own port or use someone elses to avoid that unnecessary risk. Card luck while having 8 cards and only playing 2 can still sting you, especially in 4 player game and especially for a big ship placement as spots are so limited for these, these are big points and this will hurt I'm just not sure yet how much.

RATING: 9
Amazing game, I love the shared resources system. Pretty much everything you do will be helping both you and at least one opponent, so the key seems to be to put yourself into a position that you're getting helped a lot and that when you do things you're not helping others too much. I definitely don't think I have a great grasp on this yet but it's so interesting that I want to keep playing and learning how it works. The symbiosis between the players is amazing. Sometimes the cards can be a bit frustrating if you don't get what you need, but that for me is a minor complaint here, and I don't think it would work if anyone could build anything anywhere so there needs to be some system. The foreign market though I really don't like, in particular the fact that you could just be wasting your action and throw away a card for literally no benefit is pretty rough, especially since this comes down at least somewhat to chance.

RATING: 9
Fine economic game from Martin Wallace, set in England (Lancashire) at the dawn of the industrial revolution.

I am very impressed with the game. Its economic model is pretty advanced, the rules need a bit of wrapping one's head around (but quickly become clear when playing), there is plenty of strategy and a strong feeling of history (which, to me, really makes the game stand out when compared to some of the more generic economic games on the market). The game also moves along quickly.

For me, the best game from Essen 2007 of the ones I have tried.

Current Rating: 8.0
[hr]
2015-05-01

It has been almost 5 years since I first played Brass. In those days I was a different player, new to the hobby and I couldn’t appreciate Brass for what it truly was, a truly marvelous game. To this day, I have played around 60 times and I still won’t say no to a game of Brass.

It has some luck of the draw, but 9 out of 10 times, the player who planned the best will be the winner, that is, the luck factor is low. Easy rules but each decision is important and has far reaching consequences. All of this is not apparent on the first (or second) play.

I’m a bit sad that the game didn’t get any official expansion.

Definitively best with 4 players, but I won’t say no to a 3 player game :D.

Bottom line, a great game, perhaps (along with Age of Steam) the best I have sampled from Martin Wallace and also one of the first (perhaps the very first) of his games I ever played.

RATING: 10
A lot of interesting decisions here.  Theme is very well integrated with mechanics.  Not easy to figure out on first play, however.

I'm more impressed by this game as I gain more plays.  Interaction is high and there are multiple paths to victory.  Restrictions on tile placement make this something of a positional game.  Cards introduce a potentially frustrating randomness in which one cannot take all the actions one wants, often a good thing in games but feels a little arbitrary in this one.

RATING: 8
Rating has increased from a 6 after a second play with the *correct* rules. In my first game, the teacher forgot to mention that development costs 1 iron per tile. Thus, both coal and iron works seemed worthless during much of the canal phase, skewing my perception of game balance and dynamics. Playing correctly revealed a variety of strategic options from the start that made the game much more enjoyable. My one concern is that taking at least one loan early - if not two - seems almost a precondition for victory. The advantage of having enough money to be flexible in your tactics seems to be huge; I'm not sure how one could manage this without borrowing money early. If so, that will keep my rating from going much higher. I'm not keen on games that force certain plays; if the game is well-balanced, players who don't take loans should have an equal opportunity to win. I'd love to hear from someone who has won without taking loans in which other comparably experienced players took them.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Brass is a game of building industries and networks, producing resources, and generating income. But, it does none of these things in the usual expected ways. You don't sell resources. You don't always need to be connected in a network to move some of the resources. A network does not give you income. You don't need to pay back loans (you take an income hit up front instead). 

The five different industries in the game all behave quite differently and combined with the canal/rail connectors this sets up a complicated web of relationships and player options. While there is a lot of meat in the strategy of your industries, the use of cards for actions adds a nice tactical element without damaging the game with excessive randomness.

In short, excellent stuff. The rulebook is more opaque than usual and the special laws that govern each industry makes some of the rules easy to forget or misinterpret.

RATING: 10
As a decades long fan of economic games, this to me feels like the single best Martin Wallace design and my favorite crunchy business game.  I feel Automobile to be an outstanding model of the dealership system (as someone in an industry with dealerships), but the game bogs down in cumbersome math toward the end.  I feel you are rewarded for slowing the game with some intensive what/if calculations to max/min your position.  Tinner's Trail is solid, but not quite as compelling to me (though I own that too, and recommend it).

Brass is a fabulous and balanced design right up there with Power Grid/Funkenschlag, far faster than an 18XX game, and marginally superior to even the best recent business sims that I know (it is a little different, but I have to offer a quick shout out to La Granja and the well known design brilliance of Caverna).  

You may find the initial hands of cards to be challenging, but remember all your options including the use of any two cards as one wild card.  A bit of practice will show paths to work through a difficult card set.

Consider the electronic copy to learn the game, and start converting people to this classic business game.  Maybe we can get the French map published more broadly!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Games like Power Grid and Stone Age are what might be called 'transparent' economic games - after you play once you pretty much understand how it works and the aim after that is to optimise your gameplay (which may take many many more plays).  Brass is an opaque economic game.  By 'opaque' I mean that its one of those games with a lot of interlocking mechanisms which is difficult to sort through to find a good strategy.  Even after several plays (and certainly after one play) you are still thinking, "how does this work?", not in the sense of understanding the rules, but in understanding the relative values of things - your best move is very unclear at most points in the game.  Games like Brass require a LOT of plays in order to 'get it'.  They are the sort of games where a new player CAN beat a moderately experienced player.  They are games where someone wins and someone loses and no-one can really put their finger on why.  My preference is for the more transparent game, although I am happy to play opaque games as well and would gladly play Brass again.  My only other comment is that Brass seems a little long.  Brass probably overstays its welcome by just one or two turns.

Quote from Martin De Jong which I would agree with:  "I must say I'm a bit surprised to see this game rank so highly, although I can explain it quite well: it is a growth game, it offers multiple viable paths to victory, it is low on uncontrollable luck, and it is repetitive."  I would add that Brass will generally not suffer from a runaway leader problem and (more importantly) it does not appear to have a runaway leader problem.  People seem to like that.  This is not to say that Brass is a bad game - its a good game, but a top ten place just seems a bit high to me.  Its the sort of game I would expect to see at around number 100.

RATING: 9.9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I enjoy games where the players build up a location together but in competition. Le Havre and Caylus both do this well, but the "fees" variety of worker placement doesn't quite satisfy like Brass does. Here, you have scoring incentives to bind cities together via canal and rail, but doing so may create an opportunity in that city that will be snatched up before you can capitalize on it. Building canal and rail connections will also impact the flow of coal, perhaps making your own mines more convenient suppliers. Or, building new mines might curtail demand for an opponent's more distant mine. New factories will require iron and coal for construction, which will raise demand, perhaps creating an opportunity for an opponent. And as experienced players know, consuming iron for R&D can start the vultures circling. 

Your placement options in Brass are restricted by a hand of cards, something which rubs some players the wrong way. As a long-lapsed Mt:Ger, hand-management is hardwired into me. The 8-card hand in Brass provides just the right balance between immediate tactical play, an ongoing 4-turn projection, and the ability to tweak, adjust, and respond. 

I've upgraded my rating to a 10 after nine plays. The depth of play and integration of theme are top-tier. 

See also: [b][url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/401927]My Strategy Poll for Experienced Players[/url][/b]

Update (3-9-10): I haven't played this in a few months, and the reason is that I'm worried that the actual experience of Brass is a small bait-and-switch with my "platonic ideal" of Brass. It seems crystal clear to me that the 4P game rewards taking loans hand-over-fist. Income building goes uphill and against the grain. The reverse is true of a 3-player game, which is a full four turns (8 actions) longer. A couple of loans can jumpstart an engine that far outstrips profligate loan-taking. Perhaps 15 plays doesn't make me an expert but I think both of these observations is strictly true. 

I think I want Brass to be a single game that compromises between the two extremes, instead of one with such drastically different settings. I don't know what this bodes for the future. Hopefully it is not the game's Achilles Heel for me.

RATING: 8
I usually enjoy Martin Wallace's economic games (while I'm not so much into his conflict packed Warfrog stuff) and this one is no exception. It is a clever mix of his usual elements: Few actions per player round, income track, loans, changing turn order, network building. Remarkably, there are no auctions this time (as in Automobile). It also has the usual rough edges of his self-developed games. Why does ore fall from the sky while coal has to be transported on canals or railroads? Why do you need 8 cards in your hand when they don't matter in 80% of the decisions? But all elements together form a game that leaves you thinking about better strategies in next play. More cotton production, earlier development instead of early cash-in, a railroad strategy? Various ways seem possible. Tactical optimization is needed as well ("No, I'm missing one money to do that!"). More play is wanted but not so easy to get. Startup investment for new players is high and with averagely slow players expect 3-4 hours for a game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I could be mistaken after just a few plays, but Brass seems to have a much different nerve from Age of Industry. The theme comes into much sharper relief.

In AoI, it can feel like you're not executing a strategy as much as successively hunting for efficiency gains among an open array of balanced options. In Brass, the era shift breaks things up and you need to think about your current and future options because more than just a few coins could hang in the balance.

In Brass, hand management matters: you can't take anything for granted and there are meaningful availability and accessibility concerns. The downside is that in Brass, hand management matters: you could be discarding useless cards for several rounds and [i]still[/i] not have useful ones once the draw pile is exhausted.

I don't like the random foreign market mechanism; it seems at odds with the rest of the design. You could perhaps replace it with a statistically representative succession of non-random sales?

Coal and iron are more fundamentally distinct in Brass, although the various extra maps in AoI have quirks that play around with these resources for extra variation.

AoI accommodates five players, is designed for expansions and is developed to greater polish, which probably explains why it feel less tight, more generic and more forgiving. Brass has one specific scenario, but if it's good enough, do you need others?

Poker chips worked well for money. Put those stacks in the boxes without fear of one spilling into the other.

Minor niggle: mistaken perception of who's in the lead affecting the outcome when building over iron and coal in the late game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10

RATING: 10
Outstanding complex logistic and economic strategy game. Money management is crucial. When taking loans, take them early and consider taking the maximum of 30 to avoid wasting too much actions. Don't forget to develop at least a couple of times. Try to keep a presence on the board after the Canal Period. Player order can be important, keep an eye on this when timing certain actions (like building cotton mills and selling cotton). Play in function of the cards you get and adapt when necessary. Building rail track is a great source of victory points.

RATING: 8
PLUS
Ciekawy inowacyjny koncept: zbudowana infrastruktura musi służyć także przeciwinikom, żeby dawała sukces. Kilka ciekawych mechanizmów (degresja dochodów; kredyty i ich spłata wkomponowana w system dochodów; kolejność graczy), niezła dawka interakcji i fenomen, że z czasem na planszy można robić coraz mniej a zarazem coraz więcej (inni gracze blokują pola swoimi budynkami zarazem umożliwiając inne akcje poprzez kożystanie z tych budynków).
MINUS
Niepotrzebne odchylenia od konceptu podstawowego, przez to niepotrzebne reguły na wyjątki.
SUMA
Za pierwszym razem gra koncepcyjnie trudna do pojęcia ale bardzo wynagradzająca i motywująca do wymyślania nowych metod grania w następnych partiach.

PLUS Interesting and innovative concept: your built infrastructure must serve your opponents as well in order to yield profit. Some interesting mechanisms (income degression; credits and paybacks implemented in the income system; players order), some interaction and the phenomenon, that with the game progressing the options on the board decrease and increase at the same time (facilities built by opponents occupy spaces but also make other actions possible by being used).
MINUS
Some unnecessary deflections from the basic concept creates unnecessary exception rules.
SUM
First play may be difficult and the game concept hard to grasp but it is very rewarding and motivates thinking about future games and different playing methods.

RATING: 8
Just bought this excellent heavier Euro game.  The first "economic" game I own, but perhaps not the last.  I have not yet figured out how to make the shipping strategy work in my two plays, so I think it's time I tried a production/rails strategy next game. Watch these comments next week. * NOTE: The Shipyards are overwhelmingly tempting.  I have succumbed yet again (and lost).  I begin to despair of escaping their lure...  I have also raised my rating to an 8.
* UPDATE: I have played again, and successfully avoided shipyards altogether in favor of an aggressive cotton mill strategy.  I won!
* UPDATE: I was doing well, with my "build a few mills and ports, then ship all at once" technique.  Then I succumbed and built a shipyard. I LOST!

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Well after playing 1830 I see some shortcomings to this design.

I won't talk about the rules or odd things. but in a game that is about timing and handmanagement the handmanagement aspect can be very cruel. 
In the entire game I had only ONE opportunity to build a port (or I had to sacrifice another action which I should have done probably). I didn't draw port industry cards and I only had once a city where you could build a port which i did. To be honest I drew a city but before it was my turn the port was taken. 
being forced into cotton mills because of my cards the fact that the distant market only managed to ship in both rounds three times didn't help me much either. 
And being hopefull I developed cotton and ports. Ports to no avail. Cotton was good for me.

Anyway handmanagement and distant markets are two parts of the game that have some luck in it.  And in a game that is part planning/ part taking opportunities when they present themselves I thought it was too much.

I need further plays to be sure. 

Now what I really liked about the game was that you can play with a loan strategy or an income base strategy. I'm impressed how balanced they appear. That's why I give it an 8 at the moment.
 Bad luck in distant markets (drawing the one tile that doesn't allow you to produce, and lose the game for that reason) and bad card draws have it at a 6.5.

In retrospect instead of producing I could have build railroads and win and that is another thing I have trouble with you can perfectly count the number of points each player will score making it easier to take the right (or safer) action but it takes a pretty long time.

I think next time I'll probably try to keep track of that.

Update third play.

still feel there is luck required. Certainly the last two cards; can you do anything with them or are they only to be used for connections, upgrading or producing.
In the canal era these aren't too bad but since the board is so filled up at the end of the rail era it plays a part.

I still give the game the benefit of the doubt allthough I just rated it under an 8 now. It can all be about handmanagement.


Personal feeling : Perhaps handmanagement is something I don't like. Games like 1830 and Indonesia I prefer over Brass. TTR is another game that has handmanagement and it works because it is pretty light. 1960 is another game that features handmangement that I have played but the game is much more card driven than Brass I feel.

So to me the question is : "Is Brass a game if you would take out the cards?"

Let me rephrase would Brass work if you were allowed to do the actions you wanted to do without using the cards.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
An excellent economics connections game (canals and trains) that is very different and very original.  However, it's got a learning curve to it and it has lots of fiddly and meaty rules here and there and plenty of little details and difficult to learn from scratch.  It's highly recommended to be taught by someone who is very experienced and knows all the intricacies really well.  Brass is essentially two games in one (using the same board twice in slightly different ways) and using the same building generators.  Everyone starts with the same capacity of building tile stacks but strategies and opportunities can vary greatly.  The first half is the canal phase and the second half (consisting much more of your score) is the train phase.  The economics aspect of the game lies with the players trying to increase income by building ports, coal, iron, cotton and shipyard companies.  The only knock is that it can create analysis paralysis with certain players, which would bring the game length to a slow crawl, adding an extra hour or two.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Another excellent economic engine from Martin Wallace, this time without implementing the auction mechanic.  I both appreciated and was frustrated with the cards limiting where you can build your buildings, and it was even more aggravating when I chose to throw away cards to build track/canals that I should have kept for later use.  In this game, its important to anticipate where you think much of the building will take place and save the appropriate cards for proper use while using junk cards to build track or develop.  This was one game that I never thought I would be interested in trying when I saw a group of friends attempt to learn and play the game at the same time with no veterans of this game around midnight and just struggle with it but it goes very smoothly when you have someone who knows the game explain the process.

RATING: 9
My first-ever 10 rating. At first it was an 8 because I thought the randomness was too high with the cards and the distant market, but now I see you can mitigate most of it through clever play.

Unlike other economic games, other players aren't going to be kingmaking, but you still have a lot of player interaction, using each other's infrastructure, but jockeying for position at the same time.

I love that it's a fixed-length game (16 turns at most), and that you need to strike a good mix of strategy and tactics.

Update: Back down a notch. It's too constrained and formulaic for it to be a 10. Maybe Age of Industry will take its place after more plays.

Also too tight for the randomness in the cards and what other players do. One bad incident (cards, other players moves) and you're out of the game for good.

RATING: 9

This was my first Wallace game, and I understand the hype.  The whole system, with its dozens of niggly rule exceptions, does come together to create something weirdly organic and satisfying.  The pleasures of this game are very hard to describe, but they're deeper than most; it's simply a rich gaming experience, one that seems likely to stand up to repeated plays and exploration.

RATING: 10
Have the EGG 2009 edition, the EGG Deluxe 2015 edition, and waiting for the Roxeley edition.

Update: This game is pure brilliance. If any game really deserves a 10, it is Brass. The learning curve is a bit long, but so worth it. The rules themselves have a few gameplay exceptions that can be easy to forget but overall rules are actually not hard. What makes this game brilliant yet needing several learning plays is how the relatively simple rules interact to create so many tough and interesting choices.   I'm glad I have a group who were as willing to dive into the nuances of this game as I. If you do not have the deluxe edition coins from EGG, get them. And print out one of the player boards found in the files section. These enhance the game experience immensely.

As a side note, Age of Industry doesn't even come close to Brass for me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Take part in the industrial revolution!

A very strategic game.

Rules heavy, but into the second game I no longer noticed it, and was just playing the game.  I was concerned this aspect of the game would be a reason I may not like it, but that was not the case.

In Brass, you plan a strategy early on you would like to execute to build a network of constructed industries.  One cool aspect is that each industry (represented by a tile) does not score until the goods it produced (iron, coal, cotton) are used or sold.  Then you "flip" the tile to indicate it is profitable/working and you earn the VP's and increase your income level.

While you have an overall strategy, you should also watch to see what is in demand making it a tactical game as well.

I like the turn order mechanism - the person who spends the least money in a turn will go first next time around (whoever spends the most goes last).

There is a good deal of interaction between players, both in taking opportunities/connections, but also needing to use each others' goods and ports in order to develop your own.  If you always avoid using opponents' goods/ports so as to not help them, I think you will more often lose.  This is pretty unique.

A downside might be the rules - this is a heavy game with many mechanics, and some exceptions to rules.  The number of rules makes to the game less accessible to people who like lighter games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
After one play, I was already looking forward to the next play. That's always a good sign. There's lots of room here for strategy and multiple paths to victory, and just enough randomness in the variety of cards to ensure players don't try the same strategy over and over.

There's a rumour going around that this game has complicated rules; it doesn't. Once you understand the narrative concepts (cubes represent long-term delivery contracts, coal is used in vastly greater quantities than iron) there's nothing that's particularly difficult to get your head around. I'll admit explaining the rules is a little trickier, but you can always print out a rules summary and use that to assist you.

RATING: 7
Rating based on 4 players.

This was my first heavy-ish economic game, and while it was a bit bewildering at first, by the end it all made sense and I could see more of the intricacies. While the overall strategy is somewhat dictated by the cards you draw, there's still a fair amount of space to maneuver with plenty of decisions to make. There's very little direct interaction, but other players' choices will strongly affect what you can do. It's not a game I would feel like playing very often, but I enjoyed it.

RATING: 9
2017 Top 100 Favorites of All-Time: #16

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Brass is an outstanding board game. While there are usually only a few optimal actions to take each turn, your choices are by no means limited. You must be consistent, but you are also required to diversify your approach throughout the game. Consequently, Brass is quite the brain-burner. Tough decisions have to be made every turn, and AP is sure to set in at some point. Brass no doubt has its drawbacks: a cumbersome/poorly edited rulebook, lengthy gameplay, and its inherent inaccessibility to novice gamers. This is NOT a gateway game, nor is it a middleweight. It's a big game with complex mechanisms. It's one of the best I've played. If you have the patience and the experience, Brass can be a rewarding challenge. Incidentally, the components and general production quality are top-notch.

Update 11/14/15: 

After not playing Brass for over 2 years, I was excited to get it back to the table again--esecially since I just upgraded to the "deluxe" edition. The new edition is mostly the same, but the coins and box insert are a nice touch. The gameplay is simply fantastic. It gives you that feeling before the game, of "oh, okay, I'm excited now!" The rules are a bit heavy, and it's hard to remember everything if you are new to the game (or haven't played in a while). That said, the rules are perfectly fine and comprehendible, unlike a game like Robinson Crusoe. I still love Brass and hope to play it again soon.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
3 / 5 - Good

What I like about Brass is that it brings some genuine thematic feeling to the economic games genre, which renders it fairly unique. It is also undeniably deep. Unfortunately learning and playing the game is so vastly counter-intuitive (it's not that complex, it just messes totally with the sort of cause-and-effect chains usually seen in games) that I fail to see why, when there are soooo many games of this type around, I would spend the required time with Brass in order to get to grips with it properly. As usual with economic games, I'd rather play Puerto Rico instead.

RATING: 9
So far I've only played with the 2 player variant

What I like:
- Being able to use other people's resources, but it also helps them get income and VP
- The cards help limit choices a little bit to help reduce AP, but still provide enough openness to do what you want to do.
- The first half of the game is more focused on building up an income, and the second half is focused on scoring points.
- Some route building without being too open like in train games.

What I don't like:
- Theme is a little dry, but not that bad
- Game length is a little longer, so harder to get to the table
- Lots of rules make it hard to teach to others, although I don't think they're THAT bad.

RATING: 10
It took me over a year to get this to the table, but it was worth the wait.  It's become our group's favorite game.

The theme is astonishingly well-integrated into the game.  Along with Twilight Struggle and 1960 this hits just the right balance in that department.  Everything in the game has a thematic reason for existing, but the gameplay was not sacrificed to strengthen the theme.  Whether or not any given player will find the theme even remotely interesting is another question--personally I find it very interesting indeed.

This gets a 10 because not only do I love playing it but I actively try to get it to the table.

RATING: 6
I rather liked it. Of course, I played it horribly badly, but I liked the package altogether. Competing networks with rather severe blocking, and all sorts of delicious synergies and parasitism. A mean game that wasn't as complex as I feared. The two periods is also a very welcome and interesting element. You set the scene so to speak in the canal period, and then you reap what you sow in the rail period.

My only negative concern is the cards. They felt very constrained at times but I think that is the point, what I didn't like is how easy they can seem to screw you completely (albeit mitigated by good play, but then again, what isn't for any game of (some) skill) - but it is why I don't think Brass can ever surpass Age of Steam for instance even if it does a lot of things I like.

RATING: 8.5
I have mixed feeling for my initial play.  It's a unique take on a pick-up-and-deliver train game where players need to pay attention to the supply and demand that is generated through play.  One's actions on the board are somewhat dependent on the cards you hold.  In this way the game can be somewhat tactical at times.  My group never really moved that far up the income track and in fact those that concentrated on generating income fared poorly which is counter-intuitive with this sort of game.  However, I feel that this is also due to inexperience as it has a steep learning curve.  It certainly feels like a Wallace game so if you like his designs I'd say it's well worth checking out.  I look foward to playing again and seeing what I missed the first time around.  UPDATE: raised rating 1 point: there's a lot you can do with the cards and numerous routes to victory.  It's strategic but rewards bursts of tactical play well.  Very competitive and plenty to think about.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Played once with four players and twice with three.  

This is a game with a ton of rules; it took us an hour to get through all the rules, and even then we weren't clear on all of them (forgetting minor ones throughout the game).   A player aid would help greatly.  The game itself took three full hours to play, and given the amount of difficult decisions to make, I can't see the game time being reduced by much.  

The game is relatively simple in idea: players build technology in cities or connections between cities, and then get points based on how their technologies are used and how big the cities are that they connected.  

The meat, and it is delicious meat, is in the details: a number of the resources needed to build buildings are only available from other technology cards.  You need to use the resources, but using them gives other players points.  The game also has two ages, and so a lot of the technologies you build in the first age are wiped off the map, scoring no points in the second round (unless you build techs that stick around).  And choosing where to build things is difficult: you can only build things based on the cards in your hand, and while there is a lot of flexibility, planning your moves based on your cards is essential.  

There is a ton of meat in the game, and it seems like the board changes so much as you play that it should have lots of replayability.  My only concern is the time: I can't see the rules getting cut down much, and it is a full 3 hours.

[Update: After playing another game, this time with 3 players, I'm even more happy with the game.  Widely divergent strategies (high-income vs. boats vs. cotton shipping and low income) ended up with very close scores in a tooth-gnashing game.  I've raised my rating to 8.5]
