RATING: 10
Even though I'm closing in on 100 plays, I [i]still[/i] want to play this stellar game. Yeah, I've hit the century mark with other games, but those were all quick 20 minute affairs, whereas Brass clocks in at 2.5 or more hours face-to-face and spans about a week online. No other game has given me this much enjoyment over such an extended period of time. Brass has officially earned my first and thus far only 10 rating.

[b]50 Plays Ago:[/b] I'm coming up on 50 plays, and I'm shocked to find myself still interested in playing this fantastic game. It took me a dozen plays just to become basically competent, then probably another dozen plays to get my groove back after a long hiatus. The third dozen ironed out some forgettable kinks in my game, and this last dozen has been a glorious stretch of 1st and 2nd place finishes in competitive 4-player games.* That's quite a stretch of challenging play! I'm pretty sure the shine will wear off over the next fifty plays, forcing me to drop my rating, but Brass would still be the first heavy Euro that I played a hundred times. My highest compliments to Mr. Wallace for creating such a stunning game!

* The 3-player game is not where it's at. 4 players all the way.

RATING: 8.5
Brass is a deep, strategic game with a great wide difficult decision space.  The rules are fairly straight-forward, but it's hard to see know what you're supposed to be doing or whether you are doing it well.  It's a nut that's hard to crack - so it is quite challenging.  I think there's a lot of replay value in tactically dealing with the random card draws.

On the downside, the graphic design is really lackluster.  The production *quality* is good, but the aesthetics are boring.  You spend 2 hours placing tiles that look like every other tile. Sometimes you flip the tile to the other side (which mostly looks the same) - who thinks that is exciting?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A game for Euro-heads, much like AoS.  For Brass, Wallace has abandoned his trademark elegance in favor of complex systems and unintuitive gameplay.  Everything feels very random and a tad chaotic (due to the cards), and the players who can see through all of that the best will do well.  If, however, like me, you need the first half of the game to learn the ropes, you will be neatly hung by them.  Like Tinners Trail, there's a well thought-out economic engine in place.  Unlike Tinners Trail, though, it's been smothered with Eurogunk.  One of the many pleasures of a Wallace game is marveling at how the game system models the chosen theme (mining tin or copper in Cornwall, defending or conquering Byzantium, the Peloponnesian War), but the model here is opaque and the game suffers for it.

This being a Wallace game, I'd play it again, now that I've stretched my brain around the system. But aren't there other, more dynamic, more exciting, less excruciating Wallace titles in print?  I do think so.

RATING: 10
Brass is an all around excellent game.  Very deep, with lots of rewarding strategies and important tactical decisions.  There is a lot to wrap your head around in this game, and the fact that I feel that there is still much to learn really makes the game appealing.  I like the way that you can specialize in what you build and produce, but that the game situation always seems to be such that you want to build everything.

There are a few niggling issues, such as the weird Birkenhead rule, which are more annoying than anything else.  It can also be frustrating when, through the way the cards come out, someone can completely lock up Barrow-In-Furness or Birkenhead, thus ensuring that they build the ship there.  I have won once without building any ships, so we'll see if this is really an issue or not.  If you like deep, heavy games, this is definitely one for you.  An instant classic.

RATING: 9
(I own Pegasus Spiele 'Kohle' edition)

There is a lot to like about the game. Cards limit your options just enough so you must adapt your plans accordingly but they are not limiting you too much. You must plan ahead your moves to make most of your precious actions. There is enough to think about while playing and also always so pleasant balancing between right mixture of income and points with your moves. There is also a bit of "co-operation" with other players. Sometimes you're able to build your buildings in such way that other players are more or less forced to use them and therefore give points and more income to you. 

I especially liked how the game has 2 different eras but they are still linked to each other in a subtle way. Most of the buildings (and all connections) in the first era will be gone by the second but not all. Some buildings can be built in the first era such that they give points twice or they just might be there to give you better position for second era. This is a bit similar to Amun-Re except you benefit from everything you have build on board at the start of a new era. Overall this is an excellent economic game.

Works equally great with 3 and 4 players. I'd still prefer full 4 players because of greater turn angst there is.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
In a ruleset barely comprising six chock full pages, players are instructed about how to earn money and prestige by building and trading cotton in Northern England with the emerging technology provided by the industrial revolution; they have to slowly establish their own network of cotton plants, mines, shipyards, ports and iron works, connected by canals in a first phase and then by rail in the second and final phase.

The plot sounds very familiar to experienced German game players; nevertheless, Brass isn't the standard optimization game with a historical topic tagged on but a work of art. It seems that the game has been constructed on the basis of the plot:  every single aspect of it (dependencies among different buildings and materials, historical development, supply and demand issues) has been translated into the play in a more or less streamlined fashion. 

Yet again, Brass isn't a wargame - there are some exceptional little rules here and there, but they're all pretty understandable given the nature of the game and can be assimilated easily within its water tight system. Both phases of the game develop in a radically different way even though they follow the same principles. The way steady income and loans are implemented is especially elegant. Furthermore, there's much less luck here than in a wargame: even though the mostly tactical play is based on managing a hand of cards, the luck of the draw is minimized by the stunning flexibility with which cards can be used.

I recall no other game where the thematic background and a finely crafted set of rules are married as successfully as in Brass. It's long, demanding and it might take some effort to digest the rules at first, but at the end of it I couldn't care less - it feels like just having finished a good period novel.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.4
Altro bel gioco di Wallace! Un ferroviario riuscito (anche se a mo avviso non paragonabile ai 18xx o a Age of Steam, sia per profondità che per bellezza). Il gioco si divide in due fasi (periodi), nella prima i trasporti si basano sulla navigazione; sarà importante mettere delle solide basi per la seconda fase, rimanere indietro come punteggio non è così importante quanto essere ben posizionato sulla plancia. Nella seconda fase, con il potenziamento dovuto al trasporto ferroviario, si raccoglieranno i frutti di una buona pianificazione. Determinante sarà la produzione e la richiesta di carbone e acciaio. Brass è un gioco strategico e con diverse possibilità di vittoria; trovo nella mappa il vincolo di longevità con un paio di vie di commercio strozzate. Si potrebbe/dovrebbe pensare ad altre ambientazione (altre mappe come exp) per rendere le partite sempre nuove. Componente fortuna presente la pesca delle carte può seriamente condizionare una partita.

Couple plays of this so far, both 4-player.  What a fabulous game!  Even with the horrible learning curve.  Our first game took almost 5 hours w/ various kid-related interruptions and when I decided to review the Commonly Forgotten Rules section, we had broken like 5 of them. In our group, we are having trouble remembering to always play a card for an action and to check the coal supply when building with a city card.

Our 2nd game really brought out the strategy and timing issues as all 4 of us had 1 or 2 unflipped cotton mills at the end.   The foreign market was all tapped out and we realized it wasn't worth building another port just to have someone else gain even more points from immediately selling cotton through it.  If I had gone heavy cotton mill while the others were still fighting over the level 2 shipyards (the other 3 all ended up w/ one spot), I maybe could have beat them to the rush.  I ended up scoring 70 off of tracks and only lost by 5.  3rd place was only 3 pts behind me and the player burned the worst by the cotton mills was 13 behind 3rd place.  

So great strategy still emerging.  Lots of market timing and balancing $ vs. victory points.  Great purchase although the learning curve and infrequency of playing partners with hours of time will sadly prevent this from getting played as much as I would like.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Wow! Martin Wallace on top form.

Like PR and St Petersburg Brass is an "Income/VP engine building" game where you need a decent income to have any chance of winning, but at the same time you need to generate points to win. So you're constantly having to weigh up whether you're going to build industries which generate cash but provide few victory points (such as coal mines), or vice versa (e.g. shipyards).

The game plays more like Power Grid or RRT because network building is a key mechanic, only with a more developed economic model. There’s also some hand management and resource management (as well as the obvious money management). If you like that sort of thing then you will like Brass.

It’s currently one of my favourite games, but the money is really crappy (tiddlywinks) so I replaced them with poker chips.

Bought Apr 2008

RATING: 9
Martin Wallace's masterpiece. Brass looks extremely dry and dull (it kinda is) but the experience holds so much more than cube-pushing and bland victory-point mechanics! In short, Brass is an economic simulation game set during the Industrial Revolution in England. While that sounds like every boring historical Euro about medieval families and wood gathering, Brass is unique in theme, strategy and concept. The secret to doing well in Brass is timing: exploiting "the market" (your opponents) and supplying resources for their industries, cleverly propelling you to victory. There has been an argument that Martin Wallace's games are the least and most thematic of today's game designers. From my view, Wallace dispenses with the flavor text and cheap theatrics of other historical Euros and instead builds the theme directly into the core mechanics of the game, creating a more believable and enriching experience than your typical-scramble-for-victory points Euro. You'll be thrown into the mind of an enterprising industrialist, tactfully building a network of coal, iron, cotton mills and canals for the most money and prestige. While the rulebook and the game itself can be clunky and counterintuitive at first, after one or two rounds, game play should flow well and strategies should emerge quickly after the third. (I would still recommend playing with someone who has played it once or watching a game play video before, though. It was a bear to learn.) Let me say this: Brass is undeniably Wallace's best game and maybe the best economic game. Ever. I'll explain: there are very few games that let players choose and build their own path to victory. While not exactly a sandbox (a free-for-all for engine-building), Brass has several strategies and tough decisions for players to make throughout the entire game. The optimal strategy is about timing: anticipating your opponents' moves, building where you're going to be needed, and knowing when the market is "dry." While the game isn't as easy to learn and accessible as London (my favorite of Wallace's before I played Brass), the experience of Brass is extremely rewarding and you'll want to play again as soon as you see the point spread at the end!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Explicação: 35 minutos.

RATING: 8

I've seen both loan-heavy and loan-free strategies work (each dependent on the situation), cotton strategies, coal/iron and shipyard strategies work, so the game balance seems good for a complex game. The one aspect I'm not so enamoured with is the sameness from game to game on the same map with the same cards, same strategies, standard openings, all coming to bear. All of which is the reason I've kept Age Of Industry over Brass - the former may not be as tightly woven, but I prefer the map variety for a similar gameplay experience.

RATING: 9
This is a complex game and in your first game, especially in the first Era, it is easy to forget about certain aspects and rules. In the first round, you must spend wisely and try and move up the money track as best you can while also placing the right tiles to maximize points, borrowing money at the correct times. Don't be too worried about helping others a little, if it helps you more. Once you enter the second Era, you must seek out your best option for making points and money, balancing the two in order to overstep your opponents. There can be a lot of downtime so chose your opponents carefully and some of the rule intricacies are easy to forget at first. However after a few plays I am hooked, one of my favorite Wallace games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game is pure excellence in boardgame design. The interplay between the different industries and the way that players can use each other's coal and iron make for some very interesting strategies. The two phase game with scoring at the end of each phase is very interesting in the way that it 'resets' the board to a certain extent and the player order controlled by the amount of money spent each turn give the players an awful lot of control about timing their moves. The way that income works is also intriguing and the fact that other players can score you point and income by using your industries means that you can’t just play the game in a silo. You’ve got to be aware of the other players to ensure that you’re industries will get used when you want them to and that you don’t end up over competing in one area when there is a niche that could be filled more easily, that would score you more points with less competition.

I have thoroughly enjoyed every game I’ve played of this so far and I don’t see that changing any time soon.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Brass gives a very satisfying feeling of participating in a diverse and developing economy. I love the way supply and demand fluctuate. Beautifully thematic. 3 or 4 players. 

Brass is a deep and brilliant game. How many games combine commodity production, manufacturing, and transportation successfully? In Brass, each player's production engine is inescapably tangled up with every other players' production.  There's no multiplayer solitaire here.

And the multitude of  possible strategies: specialization in one commodity, diversification across multiple industries, or two complimentary strategies (such as cotton and ports), all of which give victory points and income.  What ever you build, you must have synchronicity: transport links between your industries. Buying an expensive cotton mill and not being able to flip it is a painful lesson in economics (the cardboard variety). What Brass all adds up to is more potentially winning strategies than any other economic game I've played.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

The game does seem counter-intuitive at points though, and I think I'd have trouble explaining it, especially to someone who wasn't familiar with Age of Steam.  While the system as a whole is very solid, there are a ton of little rules that can be hard to remember.

2/17/10: One of my favorite euro games.  For some reason, I love the tile-flipping aspect; it gives the game a unique feel, instead of feeling like any other euro. The rulebook can be somewhat hard to unpack, but I learned it just by reading the rulebook and have successfully taught it to several people, so it can be done.  Highly recommended.

RATING: 9
On the downside, the rules continue to be sort of annoying, with all the little details one has to remember, and make the game seem much harder to learn than it should be. 

On the plus side, it's a complex game, with a lot of interesting decisions and some great ideas. I really like the 2 distinct phase and the neat balance between setting up your economic engine and growing your distribution network. The way technology is implemented is also very well thought out. I don't especially like the cards but they don't bother me either.

There's a definitely a learning curve to climb, but it can be a very rewarding experience. The strong bindings among players and the complex framework provide for a lot of interesting tactical choices each turn. Like other great games (Puerto Rico comes to mind) it's crucial to have a long term plan, but one suited for the game being played. It's equally crucial to have the ability to adapt and rethink things.

Wallace really needs to hire someone to develop his games! A virtual link?? Come on...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This an excellent game, that totally rubs me the wrong way. This could end up a 10 if I can get over what I don't like about it, or a 6 if it proves to be too frustrating. An 8 seems to fit now, as I would always want to play Brass, but I will also always expect to be at least a little disappointed.

I love how much strategy and tactics there are to consider, and it's not likely that any one way to play becomes dominant, as your best bet is to do things others don't. Lots of avenues to explore, little edges to gain. You have to make a plan and stick to it, and yet react to what happens around you.

It's really hard and often frustrating to deal with all the lovely little pulls and pushes that the game and other players provide, as you're limited by the cards you get. Oh, the cards! If there's one thing I hate in games, is rules that tell you what you can't do, rather than what you can. And the sole reason for the cards in Brass is to limit your options.

You've been dealt a hand of cards that's really difficult to put to good use. Do you a) cut your losses and make a move that's strictly worse than what your opponents are doing, just so you can make the best use of suboptimal cards, or b) throw those cards away and make a move that will be the most beneficial if the next cards happen to be better. Both will likely end in frustration.

I realize that if you're good at Brass, you can mitigate bad draws to some extent. But Brass is not an easy game. It's difficult enough if the cards work with you, rather than against you. It's a long game, too, and promises so much, so there's a lot that can be ruined by frustrating hands.

I'm not saying it's too random, or that lucky players will beat good players. One might argue that there are no really bad hands, and that the most important skill in Brass is making the most of what you're dealt. But it's simply not fun when you've figured out what you want to do and the cards tell you that no, you can't do that, you'll have to do something else, sorry. 

----
6 games in, I find the cards much less frustrating, although a bad hand can still cost you the game. Wonderful game, but more in an "interesting" than "fun" way.

RATING: 8
This is an interesting economic game, where players build factories and ship goods. The game has elements of a logistics game, but the more crucial element is budgeting and timing. In essence, you get a hand of cards. On your turn you get 2 actions, with each action requiring you to play or discard a card. You can build in a city that matches the card you have, or build with a special card in a city you have a link to from your previous things. Most of the buildings get populated with cubes. The cubes get spent when you or other players take actions, and when the building is empty, it flips and generates points and income. Halfway through the game, all the connections wipe, and players have another chance to build connections. It really does a fantastic job of capitalizing on other player's work, but still advancing your own cause.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
One 3-player and one 4-player game. Terrific game with lots of choices, action, and ways to score. Not a lot of screwage but opportunities exists. The ship builds in the 3 ports are very powerful and obvious, canals/tracks are at least as powerful but less obvious (for the 1st game anyway). Want to play again soon.

Canal Phase: Build a 2 counter in at least 2 of the 'biggest' cities and try for connections to them an the 2VP edges. Flip every 1 counter after taking at least 3 30 loans.
Rail Phase: Build connection from the 'biggest' cities. Build 2 rails/action as much as possible.

RATING: 9
A very good economic building game. Can very much see ideas from Age of Steam. It is more forgiving and the development of the 3 major industries, ports, shipping and transportation links feel thematic.

I like how the victory points are counter balanced against income, and how the game forces you to discard to either employ options or get better options. The inclusion of cards, along with what the other players are doing can force you into different plans.
The 2 phase approach with the cleaning of the board after the canal phase allows you change strategies in mid stream.

Like how there are several ways to pursue VPs which seem to be the value of what you set up during the game. A transportation strategy, Cotton/port, coal iron .... All in all a quite deep game.

RATING: 5
After one play of Brass, I'm not that interested in ever playing again. To start with, the game just looks drab and ugly, the rules are tough to digest and learn, and the lack of any sort of player aid seems a major omission. The components are also fairly lacking, in particular the money just felt very lame to me. 

But then the actual gameplay is only alright. The interaction between the players in what buildings you want to use and where to place is nice. I enjoyed that. But I found the card mechanic to be very annoying and entirely unnecessary. The majority of the time you are able to take whatever action you want as you'll have a card that will let you build whatever you want the most. But in my game I had several moments where Coal or Iron was in extreme demand and it seemed to me that the best play would be to build one of those buildings....and yet I couldn't because I could not get a card that would let me do it. So while my opponents had hands that were allowing them to take whatever action they thought was best, I couldn't. 

Now I get the argument is that part of the strategy and decisions in Brass are to learn to deal with your hand and make the best use of it, I think it's just an awful mechanic that makes things no fun for me. If the cards are only very rarely going to prevent me from taking the action I most want to, then isn't a lot of the game just finding out who isn't getting screwed by the deck? I guess the better question for me is how the game is improved by the cards--it seems a perfect mechanic to simply cut out of the game. 

Finally, I also just didn't like the fact that Coal had to be transported but Iron can just apparently teleport wherever it is needed. Seemed very strange, and overall the theme didn't help with the mechanics at all. In other words, most of the rules really made no intuitive or thematic sense. 

Overall I came away not impressed by this game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
Yet another cube pushing, economic engine game.

And as with games that require to build this to get this resource to get this resource to get this resource to finally get a VP, it suffers from the typical faults of that genre:

* Not particularly interactive -- unless you think Puerto Rico is interactive. The interaction is more consequential than intentional: You block your opponent or take a resource that an opponent needs because you really need it, not because you want to attack who's in the lead.

* There is a rich-get-richer syndrome in this one, which is exacerbated by having players share the same map. Building rules generally do not allow you to build anywhere you want: you can only expand. (The game does allow you to build anywhere, at a great cost.) Consequently, if you get shut out of the high scoring areas of the board, you're pretty much screwed.

The reviews make it sound way more complicated than it is. To win, you have to build as many of your own tiles on the board. However, for them to score, they have to be connected to one or more other tiles on the board. That's it!

Since it's a Wallace game, expect lots of convoluted rules that you will screw up during your first game. Really, this game could have been easily streamlined, which would have probably enhanced its strategic depth. The game is much lighter than most reviewers and comments would lead you to believe.

Other cons:
* The cards you are dealt with greatly restrict what you are able to do -- particularly where you can build.

* Downtime is longer than it should be because of how restrictive it is to where you can build. Compare this game to La Citta. In that game, you can plan exactly what you have to do to optimally score the most points. However, if an opponent takes an action card you need, you'll have to replan your move. Same thing with Brass. If an opponent builds on the last space you want to occupy, then you'll have to replan your move from the start.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
After one play with all new players, it went better than I expected.  We screwed up some pretty basic rules that will be corrected in a replay.  After playing it properly, my rating will likely improve.  

Brass is filled with so many options.  It's highly tactical too.  Plans formed at the end of one turn are easily thwarted by other players.  It's neat to have cards in such a luckless game too. Economic engine games are not usually well suited for a deck of cards, but they work very well in Brass.

I love that as the game happens the players only keep track of income, but income does not necessarily translate into victory points.  The player with the highest income can have the lowest score.  I love games that do that.  Losing sight of the real goal of the game can be enjoyable, but it doesn't help you win.  Since it isn't clear who is winning until scores are calculated, it's easy to think you are winning and enjoy yourself even if you are in last place.  I like that!

RATING: 10
Simply put, this is the game by which I judge all other games.  Brass is a terrifically tight, incredibly well balanced economic game that demands strategy to win, but also requires flexibility and opportunism.  While it is one of the most competitive games you'll ever play, Brass also requires you to rely on, and play off of, the strategies of your opponents.  This means that every game evolves in its own unique way, keeping the game very fresh and rewarding repeated plays.  Closing in on 100 games played online, I'm still trying to figure out how best to optimize my play, and that is what makes Brass so great.

RATING: 6
A make-work game, where the design deliberately makes the system complicated to keep you occupied. Unfortunately this also obscures the player interaction, as the effect of your decisions on other players' fortunes are often quite unclear. I'm not convinced the various sub-elements relating to material supply etc work quite right either. I'll usually try and shiftily move away when this game is proposed, though it is quite acceptable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A quite heavy economic game with a strong spatial aspect. I really like that the economy has very clever supply/demand structure: Players consume and at the same time provide coal and iron. Furthermore cotton mills generate demand for shipping. Furthermore, there is even some kind of capital market, in which you can take out loans and have to pay interests for the remainder of the game.

One on hand it is good to have a big network of connections to increase your oppurtunities to build things (and gain a lot of points with these connections), but you could also  easily piggy-bag on other players  networks for cotton shipping or getting ressources to building sites.

I really like the map, because it has some outstanding features (hub regions like Manchester, regions with ports, only limited and not easy-to-get-to  places for ship yards) and the possible connections change when switching to railroad area. 

Having two different eras is a neat twist. The first era is pretty much about setting you up (board position and income generation) for the second one, in which you score most of your victory points. However, this two ear mechanism and how to switch from income generation to victory  point generation makes this game rather hard for new players.

Update: 9 -> 9.5 After 10 plays I still like Brass a lot. Keeping the game from a perfect 10 is the chance of having really bad luck with the card draw, which may take you out of the competition for the win. That being sad, I have not had this problem in recent games (though other players had). So maybe you just have to play well enough to mitigate this luck of draw. I will pay attention to this in the next 10 plays.

RATING: 7
I played this once at our games club, back in early 2013. I'd previously played (and disliked) Age of Industry, which I knew was a fairly minor reworking of this game, so my hopes weren't high. But then, I played AoI very early in my exposure to modern games, and definitely wasn't ready for a moderately complex game such as this one. I certainly didn't hate playing Brass - and even managed to win the game convincingly, against 2 others who clearly knew the game, although I certainly don't think they knew too much about how to play well. I would actually be interested in playing again, so can justify rating this as high as 7 - but there was nothing particularly interesting going on, and I can't see the replayability being that high (despite the fact that the play is entirely driven by cards which are randomly drawn, as most of the actions can be done with any card, and those which require certain types usually have a wide choice such that you'll usually be able to do what you want from your 8-card hand). It's certainly a bit of a mystery to me why this is as high as 20-something in the BGG rankings (and I've been around long enough to remember when it was in the top 10) - almost certainly I'm missing some depths that may become clear on repeated plays. But it's unlikely I'll ever play this many more times, and I wouldn't feel like I was missing all that much if I never played again. A game I can see some interest in, and which I'd happily play again, but without being hugely enthusiastic about either.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I had a terrific time my first game.  This is my 2nd Wallace game to play, and I'm appreciating how much he manages to pack into his games.  The basic structure of the rules seems more elegant than Age of Steam--basically, each player takes a turn playing 2 cards, and some housekeeping is done after each round.  Unfortunately, this elegant structure is riddled with lots and lots of small rules that sound like they all have purposes, but it's a shame it couldn't be streamlined out.  In the end, Age of Steam comes out looking very, very elegant compared to the slew of rules in Brass. (And Age of Steam isn't *that* elegant a ruleset!)

But never mind,  after the hour or so that it takes to learn how to play Brass, things flow more or less smoothly.  And it's a terrific, original game.  It feels very very organic.  I like the way things develop over time, new technology replacing the old, but history having important impacts.  There is so much packed into this game, I feel a little at a loss to describe properly the feeling of playing it.  It is so intense that I ended up with a raging headache by the end of the game, and still I was enthralled.  There were a lot of anguished sighs from all players during play. I imagine the Circle of Suicide in Dante's Inferno sounds like our game.  My initial impression is that it's a very hard game, quite mysterious at first, but also a fine and strict teacher, as it punishes mistakes with brutal rapping on the knuckles, and lets you know exactly what you did wrong soon after you do it.  I feel much, much wiser after my first play and can't wait to try my luck again with The Teacher.

RATING: 7
It's fascinating watching the the various interlocking pieces of this game shift and flip and evolve as the game moves along. I like the income track and the way loans are dealt with. I like the logistics of building up a network and delivering goods. I like the soft reset between the scoring rounds representing different ages.

However, Brass is the exact opposite of elegant. I don't mind complex games, but I like fairly unified elements to help navigate the complexity. Having each different type of good behave differently is an example of this. Goods behave this way, except this one is different, and this one is different still. Cotton flips a port, but nothing else does. Iron instantly ships if there is space on the iron chart, but cotton takes an additional action to ship. Ships flip instantly, but ports don't. So many little exceptions makes it feel like the designer couldn't get the base game to work and had to keep putting in patches to keep it afloat.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.75
A really, really good game for fans of heavier, strategic games.  I had a lot of fun for half the game.  In the end, I have to realize that this is the poster child for that class of games that's been vexing me so badly lately: "Games I Wish I Could Love, But Can't."  Ultimately, the planning is too long-term for my brain to wrap around.  I end up inevitably doing badly at these kinda of games (18XX is the gold standard) with no clue at all where my game went wrong or how any of my individual decisions resulted in my final score. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Very tight game that forces you to plan things out, less you end up gutting your own company. Lots of interaction with other players on the board with plenty of opportunities to wreck their game. Players have to cooperate to a limited extent, but the tension of holding out with that help until the last possible moment is great.

The more I play, the more levels of strategy I see. You begin to understand the importance of turn order, of when to play your iron works (which can be easily overbuilt if they come out too early), the balance of how many loans to take and how aggressively to develop your industries. It's a rich game that can stand up to intense study.

Just a couple, minor quibbles: The core of the game is a tight, economic game, but there are a number of exceptions to the rules on top of it that require a few plays to completely remember. This game is unforgiving, so forgetting some of those exceptions can really ruin the experience at first. This game needs a few plays to fully appreciate, but it is well worth it.

Also, there is a little luck with the card draws. There are times when you are really hoping to draw a certain card and never get it. This a minor quibble though, as this luck can be mitigated with a little forethought. The bigger issue is the uncertainty of what the other players can and cannot do, which adds a little bit of hidden information.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3

---
Másodjára (=első teljes játék) már tisztult a kép. A szabálykönyv minősíthetetlen továbbra is (thaur munkája nélkülözhetetlen!). Rengeteg apró kivétel, finomság, stb. Az érzésem az, hogy két-három parti után fog igazán letisztulni. Egyébként a játékmenet egyáltalán nem bonyolult. Viszont meglepett, hogy a dicsért gazdasági játék (meg, hogy átjön a tematikája) mivolta egyáltalán nem érvényesült. Számomra eddig csalódás. Igaz, hogy full gémer a játék, de szerintem túlságosan agyonbonyolított (akkora a cselekedetek közötti választási lehetőség, hogy teljesen átláthatatlan emiatt). Jobban szeretem a sallangmentesebb játékokat. Azért még kell vele párat játszani, hogy hitelesebben ítélhessem meg.
---
Továbbra sem hozta azt, amit én vártam tőle. A játékszabály borzadalmas, ill. az apró kivételek miatt nem elegáns, nem könnyed a játékmenet.
---
Erőltettem-erőltettem, de ez a játék nem jó. Csak a helyet foglalta, ezért túladtam rajta.

RATING: 5
At first I thought that this would be far too 'round', like Power Grid, with ridiculous catch-up mechanisms and ever changing maps to distract one from the very influential plant flipping order, but not so! It has much more depth than I saw at first, & the forced sharing of resources, that kicks it up a level.

We went though phases of group think on this one--massive loans! all about Cotton! Only foreign markets! etc. The game has been robust enough to outlast all of our decisions about its logical end point.

I wouldn't call myself a Martin Wallace fan, but I do kinda like this one and AFAOS, although this one is certainly less contentious than that one, and for me isn't reliant on theme to be exciting--the mechanics are great.

For trade--I like it to play, but not enough to own, I think...copy in VG cond, only played once, Eagle games version with double sided board w/ 2p variant on the back.

RATING: 9
The theme is an instant winner for me. Maybe an interest in the industrial revolution and North West England helps. Numerous strategies for success, but a player must be flexible depending on what cards they are dealt, and upon what strategies their oponents are pursuing. Really, really like this game. Wallace's best ever?

The two player variant is worthwhle, and even though there are no shortage of games to play for 2, I don't think there is much competition for a serious economic game. 

4 player is clearly where this game is best - the competition is insane, and there is something really delightful about the struggle to find an economic niche. But three player is solid too, altho I think there may be more risk of a player's lead to snowball if allowed to dominate an industry - so don't let em.

If playing this with me, be prepared to get fed up at my shaking my head and saying "I really, really love this game" periodically.

RATING: 7.5
I have played this game twice - once  November 06 and May 07. Quite a few changes between each prototype. The second game shows improvements, but there are some tweaks required. 
The balance of the game is about right. 
The decisions to be made are numerous as the cards held don't really put any limit on what you can build once you have a base established. The cards can seem almost irrelevant - all actions that you take just require a card (any card) to be discarded. 
In the first game I played, Iron foundaries were important. You need iron to build higher value Cotton Mills, Coal Mines and Shipyards, but in the entire second game only three were built and there was still iron sitting on one foundary unused at the end of the game. I didn't build one as I didn't see the need. 
However, Coal Mines are important as railway building in the 2nd phase of the game means you need lots of coal.
Mills are certainly useful for upping your income so long as you have a port available to ship it out.
Very nice idea for a game which I am sure will be a hit on release (which it certainly was).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A very unique game, the only one I've played where it's completely about timing the spending of your money, and when you take loans. And you manipulate the turn order in order to optimize your investments. The simple but innovative income track makes the whole thing work.

On the flip side, this game has a ton of tiny rules and exceptions, that it's a nightmare to get comfortable with the language and "gotcha" moments are unavoidable (and very fatal/irritating in your first few 3-hour games). It's just too many little quirks for an average person to absorb all in one sitting. The counter-intuitive strategy also means you need 3-4 games before getting the point.

The components are cheap. FRED cheap. My box ripped really easily before a single play, the plastic chips are shitty and useless, and in the case of my copy, were strewn all over the box when I opened it, resulting in the rip, (they even made the spaces big enough for poker chips, c'mon!), the cards are of paper-thin stock, and start bending from the first time you shuffle them. 

I classified this as an economic game, but there is very little actual economic gameplay beyond the basic consideration of supplying when demand is high and taking advantage of newly extraneous supply (plus setting yourself up to be in those situations, which is very non-trivial). This game is just as much about spatial networks and turn order. What I really like is the "race" nature of the game. You're racing to get VPs in a set amount of time (actions) on some gas (income). Loans you take are like pit stops. These are probably the best and most realistic "race" mechanisms I've experienced, thanks to the strategy involved with "pit stops", and they would translate very well to a NASCAR/Formula 1 game.

Authored the definitive Brass strategy guide [thread=494294]here[/thread].

============

My main gripe with the game (and this only comes after attaining expert level): A lot of things, like the Canal Phase wipeout, Level 1s, Birkenhead, in this game are thematically/historically driven. Yet, if Wallace hadn't put in those artificial constraints, the strategic space of this game would be a lot wider, and potentially better. The game is still (very) playable, but it could have been a lot better. For example, the low-tech strategy is completely impractical, and it might have been interesting for the game to put it on even footing with high-develop hi-tech, instead of seeing how the hi-tech players outdo each other. Problem here is nothing tells the new players "don't build level-1" or "don't go into Ports if 1 other already went there unless you can beat him", and that the rules have been rigged to completely shut off your chances at winning if you try building Level 1. You can only think, "gee, I bet Wallace is trying to make a point about obsolescence", and develop them first chance you get. The alternative to this thought is that the low-tech strategy was actually erroneously thought of as viable during playtesting (I'd love to know what were the player scores during playtesting), which notion I've only entertained after A Few Acres of Snow successfully passed through playtesting. It's natural to suspect game balance flaws when a designer is this prolific.

And really, the theme isn't the most stirring one to warrant these weird constraints, either. In fact, I think theme and mechanisms are trade-offs for Wallace. Brass is the "theme" game that tries to teach you a lesson about going for level-1, while Age of Industry is the "balanced" game, with artificial constraints removed. Similar for A Few Acres of Snow vis a vis Mythotopia (though at least Brass isn't broken, since it doesn't have asymmetry, and the players balance the game by going for the overpowered strategy, and it becomes a matter of who can run it better, or if it then makes the underpowered strategy viable enough). 

Also pretty lame how nearly every Martin Wallace design boils down to you get x actions a turn and here's your supersized menu of actions. Go order it up. It's lazy design on par with Worker Placement, if that were the only thing in here. But the aforementioned income track, loans and turn-order manipulation make it worthwhile for plenty of plays.

4+4

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Played once with 3P. I can't recall Age of Industry well enough to make a comparison, so this is based of my impressions of Brass on its own. 

At its simplest, the economic system in Brass is about reading the game situation and exploiting demand in the economy. Players may go into the game with some semblance of strategy, but the actions of other players require players to be amenable to readjusting their plans. It may seem sensible to cooperate to get tiles flipped at one point, but in an instant the situation could trigger a race to build certain industries. The element of hand management also plays a role in determining the optimal actions of a player.

The two-part gameplay is interesting, and thoughs players have to try setting themselves up in the first half so that they can hit the ground running in the second half. It does seem that the first half runs a little long, and serves more of an intro for the second half where things get really interesting, but that may be inexperience talking.

There are a few aspects of the game which are less-than-positive to me. Graphics-design wise, it isn't easy to tell which tiles have been flipped at a glance. There are also tiny rules which may be mechanically sound, but they aren't easily remembered nor are they particularly intuitive. (I consider the differences between the handling of iron and coal among the trickier rules.) These may not be a problem for regular players, but as a player who is unlikely to play this frequently, streamlined and intuitive rules really go a long way. 

The biggest downside for me is the potential for downtime in this game. Subtle changes on the board often necessitate a reassessment of plans during a player's turn, and having to reassess 8 different cards during your turn leads to some significant pauses. Granted, this does not happen every round, but it happens frequently enough to make itself noticeable.

While I am not proficient nor am I particularly keen on economic games, Brass offers plenty of engaging decisions and requires a few plays to get a hang of the various strategies involved.  I can't bring myself to brush aside the game's flaws, but there is no denying that the economic system in the game is really solid and remarkable.

RATING: 8
[4 plays] Brass is a well themed economic game which has nice crisp turns and some brain burning moments. 

First time out I was in a fog the first third of this game, confused about how things worked and clueless about how to discover a good strategy. Then somewhere along the process everything clicked together nicely for everyone and we were off to the races. I thought my shipbuilding moves would secure me the victory, and in the end, I like the game better for the fact that they did not. There is no killer strategy which immediately breaks the game, you have to play smartly and keep an eye on what everyone else is doing.

Establishing a good income early is important, but unlike Age of Steam, you can apparently take the occasional hefty loan without long term crippling effects. This keeps everyone in the game, even if playing for the first time.

Pretty nice components and decent graphical design, something worth noting from this designer/publisher. Not quite up there with Age of Steam for my favorite Martin Wallace game, but still quite good. (I did not really care for the rather lifeless exercises that are Perikles, Byzantium, and Liberté - while Steam and RR Tycoon are thematic and good.)

Most recent play 1/14/13 online with Michael and Corey.

Additional comments after my second playing:
Loans are not to be feared, everyone should be doing them, just time them well. Links are the key to victory - absolutely crucial in the second half scoring. Wonderful game.

Further comments after 3rd play: Links are not the end all. Corey (with tons of online play under his belt) did some major development digging which allowed him to dominate by building very high point cotton mills.

RATING: 8
[2011-04-09]
Kőkemény gazdasági szimuláció. A szabálymagyarázat után nem sok jóra számítottam, de a játék megmutatta miért szerepel előkelő helyen a BGG toplistán. Parti közben rájön az ember, hogy nem is annyira száraz, illetve nem is annyira bonyolult, mint amilyennek a szabály alapján tűnik. Mégis jól játszani igazi kihívás benne. Igazi stratégia és persze taktikai játék is. Nagyon sok jó mechanizmus található a játékban. A bevételek, kölcsönök, az épületek egymásra hatása és persze a kártyák. Amit először úgy éreztem nagyon nagy szerencsét visz a játékba, aztán rájön az ember, hogy itt az egyik mély stratégiai elemmel találkozott. Nagyon jól kell tudni előre gondolkodni és megfelelő kártyákat jó időben felhasználni jó akciókra.
A játékosok Anglia egyik tartományának iparát fejlesztik. Bányákat és acélüzemeket építenek, amelyek nyersanyagokkal látják el a csatornákon, illetve a vasúton összekötött különböző üzemeket. A játék nem vészesen hosszú és 3-an is tökéletes élményt ad, 4-en már jobban konfliktusossá válik.
Én a pamuteladás egyik módjának szerencseszerepét érzem egy ilyen játékban kicsit soknak, illetve a játék kivitelezése lehetne szebb, bár egyértelmű, hogy nem ez volt a cél.
A gazdasági szimuláció kedvelőinek, illetve igazi gémereknek tökéletes választás.

RATING: 8
Brass is a strange game.  I didn't like it much at first.  I thought "what was all the hype about on the BGG forums?".  Then I finally got to sit down with some good friends and actually play through a complete game. And I was wowed!  Immediately after playing I wanted to play again because I saw strategy and options unfold before my eyes.

Once the game "clicks" in your head, I'm confident you will love it!  It's pure strategy, except for the hand of cards you draw.  But still, these cards I feel add to the game, not take away from it.

It's already rated pretty high by me, but if I could get more games under my belt to see more of the game's intricacies unfold (which I know are there), I would probably rate it even higher.

RATING: 8
A very cool economic building game. The first game is definitely a learning game.

More forgiving than Age of Steam, but still very fun and wonderfully thematic. The randomness of the cards keeps it from being perfectly calculable, which is good! It speeds up play, and keeps the game more interesting (I'd like some hand management, please!). There are several well-balanced ways to earn VP (which represent future potential for the company; to me, VP represents the sale value of the company at the end of the game: $10/VP).

While I prefer Age of Steam and Struggle of Empires, this should be easier to get to the table. 

Not quite a 10 for me, but pretty close!

I'd like to play again, but my first impression is the design is not simple or streamlined enough to elevate it amongst the ranks of great games.  Update:  after two complete games I'm still fuzzy on some of the rules.  I don't see the logic behind the rules and it makes Brass non-intuitive.  I think the best game designs are usually elegant in their simplicity and this is the antithesis.

RATING: 6
I'm one who can find appreciation in both theme and game mechanics. In fact I often lean more in favor of mechanics. I would rather play a dry themeless game with unique mechanics than a boring game with stellar theme. Well, dry and themeless certainly describes this game. I wasn't at all impressed with the mechanics either though. the game is really popular so I was surprised when the game played like just an overcomplicated economic engine builder. 

Each player gets two actions a turn and uses cards to drive their actions which involve building, upgrading, etc. different industry tiles in order to make the most money by end game. It didn't grab me one bit, I've played much more interesting economic and engine building games.

I haven't played the new base game titled Brass Lancashire. The version I have is simply called Brass and the component quality is atrocious. Thin little pieces of cardboard for your industry tiles and colored plastic chips for the money. Yuck!

RATING: 9
100531- 2 plays, rating: 9

To my knowledge this is my first Martin Wallace game to date, and boy what a game. As a friend of mine put it: "Martin Wallace is a special taste, either you enjoy it or you think it is dry as the desert in with you want to bury his games". I didn't find this dry at all, quite the opposite. Brass is an economic game of developing your cotton industries in 18th century England, so yes, I can see that some people could experience the game as "dry", but for me it isn't. Instead I get really intrigued in trying to make my industry wheels (Jennys) spin faster than my opponent. I dive in and all a sudden is the game ending and 180 minutes has passed. As I later lay in my bed I cannot sleep because my mind is preoccupied with coal, cotton and iron, not to forget the shipbuilding, and how to develop/produce in the most effective way. And that is not all! My opponent this far seems to have similar feelings about this wonder of a game. There are some drawbacks to the production. The rule book gave us a bit of trouble. The Birkenhead rule was one part we got wrong and even after an effort to correct it some players missed it the second time to and protested wildly that we hadn't talked about it. Well I know we did. Anyhow, the rules aren't that hard at all and  good when you understand them, and with a video review and some player aids, that is corrected.
I think this game will raise to a 10 but I want to play it at least 5 times before I make that kind of commitment. And Martin, yes you Wallace, I will buy some more of your games, because if they are even close to this good then they are worth playing for many years.

Deluxe Edition Exclusives: 70 Clay poker chips/game for money, 2 mm player mats, Foil Box edges, Black chitboard tiles, Custom Vac Tray Insert, Numbered Box.

2017 Edition:
1. New Art.
2. Removal of virtual link between Birkenhead and Liverpool.
3. Level 1 cotton mills buffed to be worth 5VP (Was 3VP).
4. There are 64 Cards instead of 66. They removed 2 cards "to streamline play and reduce a few weird edge cases". (They may include them as an option to play the original deck.)
5. 3 player game shortened and deck tuned to bring experience closer in line with 4 player.
6. 2 player rules added/included. (Well, rather than a board they remove city cards.)

"JOGO DO ANO 2007" WINNER, By http://SpielPortugal.blogspot.com

Very addictive game, one of the most brilliant games out there. A certain amount of luck driven by the cards that can easily (or not) be out-passed by players skills. That's the challenge: try to win all games even if you don't have the cards playing on your side. It's difficult to master this game but the real opportunity comes every time it is played and in different ways.
Brass plays in about 2 hours and it's one of the most intense and complex games ever made. Walace really pulled his hat off with this one. I can only imagine the hard work on testing this game. A pure Warfrog game that has a good replay ability despite its map being static. A good way to make players happy is to publish another map for this game with one or two rules changes.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I enjoy Brass a lot even though it's not the most elegant game (I like fluid and elegant games). It is immersed in Wallace's very typical exceptions to the rules and feels slightly scripted. It has a few "if's, but's and what's", which occasionally detract from the game's flow and theme.
The game's climax seems to be reached a few turns before the final one.
Initially I hated this, now I've grown accustomed and play it like it is - it's part of the game's personality.
An important deciding factor that takes place in stage 2 of the game and where players usually spend some time, is on building tracks all over the place (if at all possible) just to score some precious additional points - seldom with a specific strategic purpose besides getting those extra points. In this situation the game simply seems to ignore the theme.
Now, in spite of Brass not being a "clean affair", and any additional time spent on development and playtesting might have been put to good use, this game is thoroughly enjoyable.
The more I play it against experienced players, the more tight and fun it seems to become.
It started with a 7 rating and has grown to a 9.
To me this is usually a great indicator. Despite its minor flaws, Brass is a game I highly recommend. Plus, it can be played in 2 hours tops.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Wallace? And still not broken?!? Just kidding. Wallace's designs are beautiful in their essence. If only we could all play as he envisioned. Yeah, I'm thinking AFAoS and London. Both wonderful games. Both broken. And while there is hope for London (variant and/or playing with more than 2 players), AFAoS is doomed. High hopes had destroyed it.

Brass is, on the contrary, pure excellence. At least, I read so :-)

RATING: 9
Is it good?  yes, it's brilliant--but you have to like games with relatively brutal efficiency engines, and long-term strategy with some tactical speed requirements as opportunities arise, and therefore your ability to look downstream is particularly important including across a tech-gap mid-game.  The game is relatively long-- realistically expect a 4 hour game to start with (with 4 players) and expect it to not get much faster than about 3, if your players are thoughtful.  I don't mean suffer from AP, but take the appropriate amount of time to analyze the situation so they can play their best.

RATING: 8
Lots of odd little details (coal must be connected, iron doesn't; you can pay £5 even if the market is empty; you can ship to distant markets through an unflipped port, etc.) make the game irritating to teach and learn, especially because of the lack of an included player aid or affordances on the board.

But these wrinkles aside, it's a surprisingly simple game that moves quite quickly. Perhaps the biggest downside is that luck of the card draws is perhaps too significant in an economic game of this length. A great game.

RATING: 6.5
[FRED 2007 edition] {Industry, economic; variable-player-order, action-point, loan, epic; optimal 4p}  Brass is a typical Euro game: agonizing decisions, tight resources management and many calculations.  The good thing, for me, is that it is not a perfect information game, so AP is not a critical problem.  Brass is fun, interactive, strategic and multifaceted.  It simulates the supply and demand principle nicely.  The development aspect of the technologies and cities is a nice mechanic so that players can be more involved.  It is deep and unforgiving, not suitable for new players.  It is a typical Martin Wallace's game - the winner is determined by VP, not by earning power or cash.

Several problems prevent Brass from being classic.  There are far too many fiddly rules and exceptions (I usu. don't like that).  There are thematic incorrect rules; some rules are just counter-intuitive.  And, there are some rules / areas I think are under developed.

The rulebook contains all details but it is not well organized.  The details are scattered in different sections of the rulebook. A better player aid is needed.

The components are sturdy. But the artwork is just so-so, not interesting at all.  The board is quite busy.  A player mat shall be provided.

RATING: 9
Competitive development in the Industrial Revolution. Described as an 'opportunistic' game when I was taught it, and that word has stuck with me. You're developing your industrial and transportation base, but your industry is only profitable (income, VP) if there is adequate demand for its products, and your transportation network is needed to spread your industries but its ultimate VP value depends on how many profitable industries it connects, irrespective of who owns those industries.

RATING: 9
Strategically absorbing economic, building and transportation game set during the Industrial Revolution. The object is to develop a number of industries such as cotton milling, coal mining and ship building, and then develop a transport network through first the canal age and then the railway age to transport goods for sale. The main mechanism is card-driven where you play cards depicting either specific industries or towns/cities in the north-west of England, which allow you to carry out those developments and where. There is an enormous amount of thought that goes into each decision you make as you try to ensure optimum use of your own developments/networks, whilst keeping more than an eye on how to exploit what your opponents are doing too. I feel that there is an over-reliance on taking out loans to fund your actions as opposed to gaining income from your industries but overall the result of 'Brass' is an engrossing game which is arguably designer Martin Wallace's finest effort to date.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9

The building cards and the routes on the map provide just enough decisions and options/frustration to keep that part of the game interesting but not over bearing.  Looking forward to future plays. 2008/10/25

RATING: 9.7
One of those games you tend to think about even when not playing.  I really like how 'Brass' is played out over two historically distinct phases.  Overall, it makes for an excellent sense of developmental ebb-and-flow, in which market timing becomes a premium concern.  The other major concern, which of course is signature Martin, is all about finagling market access by building up clever networks of canals and later railways.  Tough decisions abound, many relating to cash-flow:  how many loans is going to be too many?--And to what extent am I willing to ally myself to a shared network? Really a wonderfully interactive game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
A fantastic game. Really intense economical game where players have to start from zero income and slowly build up. I feel the money is even more tight than in Age of Steam. 

What I like about Brass is that there's more options than in AoS: in the latter you just build track after track but in Brass you can choose to develop your factories or you can build one of five different kinds of buildings which all cost a different amount of money. 

Sure, there are cards  which dictate where you can build and it is possible to get a bit hosed if you don't have the right card. On positive side, though, there's no rush: if you don't have the card now, you can have it later since opportunities disappear very rarely. And you can always play two cards and only one action in a turn to build anywhere you want.

RATING: 9.5
Just an awesome, dynamic game. Very tactical.  You have to make the best use of your cards, but it doesn't feel like a 'bad' draw is devastating. You need to constantly juggle money spent compared to everyone else to ensure a good spot in the next turn.  You have to build your plan around what everyone else does, and it gets difficult.

The very next time we played,  everyone concentrated on building rails, so there was a ton of coal, and there were 2 ports that didn't get flipped.  The game dynamic follows the actions of the players, rather than the player dynamic followed the game.  

This game is all aces.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Brass is a great mind-melter of a game, but I'm going to dwell on a few things about it that bugged me.  

First, there's a completely unnecessary layer of rules complexity.  It makes the game harder to learn/teach and really doesn't add anything to the game of value.  I'd prefer it if it was more abstracted rather than trying to simulate the historical differences between shipping iron and coal, for example.  And that fucking channel...

Second, adding up scoring is REALLY finicky.  I think it'd be easy to mess that up.  

Third, the last few turns feel more like cleanup as players scrounge for points with severely limited options.  The game feels like it's already wrapped up but we're still there playing.  Maybe the game would have a better crescendo if it ended a turn or two sooner when players are still deciding between actual options?

Fourth, those slippery plastic coin tokens suck.  I think all four of us dropped at least one on the floor.  

But now I'm nitpicking.  I really did like it and hope to play again.  (But I won't buy it because I don't want to be the one teaching it.  Maybe Age of Industry...)

RATING: 9.5
Can't get it to the table, so I'm dabbling with online play...

3/24/12 - Wow. Just wow. I'm very impressed. I wrote this mini-review in my GCL. So I decided to just copy-and-paste it here. Enjoy!

--

So what did I enjoy about Brass? Here's my best effort to sum it all up:

1. Tension. This game has tension in spades. The best way to illustrate this is by examining one of my favorite design elements to the game: turn order. In Brass, a player takes two actions on every turn. Turn order changes every round based on the amount of money spent by each player at the end of each turn. The player who spends the least, gets turn-order priority in the next turn. So by spending less this turn, I can potentially go first next turn. This makes for some very interesting and powerful decision making. In most actions, you'll be spending at least a few pounds to complete the action. But there is one action that doesn't cost anything, and that's getting more money (taking a loan). So you can see what kind of great decisions develop: I want to build my cotton mill, and would like to sell to the distant market before Billy. But the cost of the cotton mill will put me after him in turn order next turn, so he could sell before me. Okay then, I'll just build this link for 3pounds then take a loan. Then next turn I'll have cash on hand, get to go first, build my cotton mill, then sell before Billy can even react. Brilliant! Now, which cards do I use?...

Very exciting stuff. Tension abounding.

2. Unique economics. I don't have a "true" economic game in my collection. I don't consider Brass to be one. It's no 18xx title, that's for sure. There's no real speculation and/or market trade. And its market is fairly abstracted with several rule exceptions for me to consider it "true". But what it does well is create an atmosphere where players aggressively compete in a tight space for the best financial positioning. There are a few markets to consider: iron, coal, and cotton sales in distant lands. The distant market for cotton is simply a race mechanic. The first couple players to sell to the distant market make the most money and have the least risk that their sales will be rejected (while demand is high). The other two are only present when players on the board aren't offering iron or coal from their industries. The variety of industries is nice, but they're all very simple--which I like.

Loans are an important part to success in this game. I can't imagine a player ever getting the money he needs to win without taking out at least a couple of loans. But the loans are also very forgiving. In most situations, you can see within one or two turns how you will pay back your debt, and usually surpass the debt you incurred for the loan with optimal play. It's also entirely possible for no players to ever pay anything to the bank since players start with enough money to build up the required industries to generate income each turn.

There are no stocks in Brass.

In short, the economic system here is very interesting, but it's not going to give a lot of satisfaction to advanced economic gamers. That isn't to say it doesn't have its place. It definitely does, but if you're looking for a deep, detailed economic simulation, you won't find it here. If you want a more stream-lined, tactical-based economic system (less focus on buy/sell), then this may be your game.

That said, it's perfect for me and my gaming level. Some day I'll be ready for more. (I'm currently considering Poseidon or 1830 for more.) for now, I'm loving what Brass offers for an economic gaming platform.

3. Engine building without the upkeep. Great engine-building designs often get fiddly. Mr. Wallace has created a low-maintenance engine-building system with the industry tiles in Brass. You build an industry and basically there is a required amount of goods each industry needs to generate/fulfill to pay it off. After that, you simply get money each turn (and some VPs at the end of the period). Simple and elegant. It doesn't even seem like you're building an engine, but you are. Industry tiles that seem crazy-expensive at the first of the game are more affordable at the end of the game. Flipping tiles (meeting its fulfillment requirements) at end-game becomes easier than it was at the first. Before another player mentioned its engine-building element, I hadn't even thought of it that way. That's how elegant it is.

There's something incredibly satisfying about flipping over several industry tiles in one turn, moving your income marker up the track, and seeing more VPs in your color on the board. It's also fun to see how everybody's networks grow to eventually devour a board that initially seems unnecessarily oversized! It's an incredible implementation of several relatively-complex ideas that all come together smoothly. And it is fun to make it all happen together.

4. Coopetition. This is not a typo. When I first read it on another review, I knew exactly what the reviewer was saying. If I hadn't played Brass, I probably wouldn't have known, and would have thought it was a typo. Brass introduces coopetition. Paul Springer talks in his UFBRT video review about how every part works together in a system of inter-connectivity, and without every part, it wouldn't work--including the players! Players who build an industry are dependent on other players to build other industries requiring their produced resources, while that second player is dependent on the first player just to build that industry that he needs. Did I just blow your mind? Good, because that's what this game has done to me so far. It has blown my mind.

--

I can't believe I let Brass sit on my shelf for so long without playing it. Now that I've got two full plays under my belt, and two more close to finishing. I feel ready to take it to the members of my family that will appreciate it--my mathy-gamer sister is going to LOVE it.

After two full plays (and two in progress online), I'm giving it a 9.5, it's immediately going into my Top 10, and I assume it will be in my Hot 10 for some time to come. I'm happy to say that what is probably a respected classic for most of you is my current HOTNESS!
[COLOR=#FFFFFF]TAGS: economic, euro, hand management, 2-4 players, favorites, Wallace, medium-heavy, route building, empire building[/COLOR]

RATING: 5
I made sure to give this a second try after not being particularly enamored after my first play, and the second play only served to confirm my feeling that this may just not be for me.  Most people seem to love this one, and I was certainly eagerly anticipating Wallace finally making another non-fighting game, since he'd been doing things like Byzantium and Perikles lately.  However, I think I'd always rather play Age of Steam over Brass because AoS is just a more fulfilling route-building game.  Admittedly Brass is very different, and has some interesting elements going for it, such as the clever way the resource market and movement works and the upgrading of buildings (like a technology tree).  I don't mind the board wiping clean in Amun-Re, but the way the Old and New Kingdoms work in Brass just doesn't sit right with me as it seems a bit too forced.  I also know I shouldn't judge the game's balance after only two players when I'm sure it was playtested countless times, but I can't see the shipyards not being the dominant strategy, so the idea of having plenty of choices seems misleading since getting your four shipyards seems like it ought to be your focus, it's certainly been the winning strategy in both games I played.  I'd be willing to give Brass a third try, but won't be begging to play it again.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Upgraded to a 10 after about 10 plays. Excellent, excellent game. Definitely my favorite of Essen 2007, even over the very good Agricola. The balance is superb, it feels thematic (to someone who knows only a smattering of the relevant history, anyway), there's plenty of interaction but it's indirect denial and making things difficult rather than in-your-face aggression, and skillful play is well-rewarded with victory points.

The randomness in the cards really hits a sweet point for me -- it constrains you just enough that you can't ignore what you have in your hand, but never so much that you feel like the cards are playing you instead, or that the luck of the draw was a big factor in the game result. Big kudos on a really ingenious mechanism there.

My only complaint is that there are enough exceptions and one-off rules and things that work this this one way and that the other that the game is hard to teach to new players. Three examples: To get coal somewhere, you need (anyone's) link; for iron, you don't. To play an industry card, you need your own link, plus resources; for location cards, you don't, but you still need resources. You can upgrade your own buildings, except that you can upgrade someone elses coal or iron when the supply is completely exhausted. Anyway, these problems go away when played with people who have played before, and they make sense when you think about them from a game-design perspective, but they do make the teaching of the game a bit exhausting and full of 'oh yeah, I forgot to mention...' moments.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[thing=28720][/thing] is a heavy economic game in which you build ports, coal mines, iron works, shipyards and cotton mills in order to gain income and points. The game has two phases: the canal phase and the railroad phase. In the end of each phase the buildings fully used are scored, and also the connections. Buildings of level 1 can only be built in the canal phase, and they leave the board when the phase 2 start.

All the constructions, when fully used, give two things: income and points. The income you take immediately. Some buildings give more of one type and less of the other. And the level of them (that goes from 1 to 4, except the shipyards, that goes from 0 to 2) change a little the amounts given. For exemple, a level 2 coal mine gives 7 of income and 2 points; a level 4 gives 5 income and 4 points.

[thing=28720][/thing] uses a smart hand management to work: there are two types of cards - the City cards and the Building cards. You use the City cards to build in the named city, even if you don't have a connection that leads there. The Building card you use to build the type of construction on it anywhere that you have a connection. Some buildings require the connection to a port or to a coal mine or a iron works, because it uses coal or iron.

On a player turn you have two actions and they are:
- building an industry;
- building a connection (either canals or rails based on phase of the game);
- develop their own industries which removes lower level industries from the player's stack in favor of higher level;
- sell cotton;
- take a loan.

The game goes for two phases, and the phases end when all the cards of everyone have been used.

[thing=28720][/thing] provides a lot of player interaction (you can, and probably will, use other players ports, coal mines, iron works and connections). The market/demand for coal, cotton and iron is simple and they work really well. Pretty straightforward. The game has a lot of tension, since the spaces start to be taken by constructions, and your options gets tighter every round, so you must consider a lot which cards to use, because some of them maybe key in order to give you some wiggle room in late rounds.

Timming is also a strong aspect of the game: when to build, is as important as to where and which. It is paramount to see the opportunities given by the demand of resources, the open spaces, and the moment to spend and when to save money in order to be one of the first to act next round (the player order goes from the player that spend the least to the one that spend the most). Every action seems connected to the next.

I really liked that the loan in [thing=28720][/thing] isn't something that will totally break your legs - a loan affects your income, but not by much. It is just another part of the game, and you will probably have to take a few of them - and, as everything else, when to take a loan is important (and usually is better to take the maximum of 30 pounds), not only, but also because when the stack of cards is depleted you won't be able to take a loan.

So [thing=28720][/thing] is a great game, one that you will have to use your thinking cap more than once.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
1 play so far. One of the most AP prone and brain burning games I've played. So many variables on the board, 8 cards with about 4 possible actions each to consider each turn, and variable turn order means you have to think about what to do A LOT. I was engaged just about the whole game though, so downtime felt TOO short at times. The person before you can screw up your plans forcing you to sit there thinking while everyone is waiting for you. 

I greatly enjoyed how the first half of the game and 2nd half were very different, but it wasn't something I was really expecting or could understand until we got to that point in my first game. The Coal demand went through the roof! I really liked the supply/demand dynamic between players and this brought a feeling to the game that was unique for me. 

I lost horribly but a large part of that was me making a critical misjudgement in the last quarter or so of the game and if I hadn't done so I would have been 3rd or 2nd instead of last. Still an interesting game!
